### ⚡ Section 6: Workflow Trigger Events - Part 2 (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [05-Workflow-Trigger-Events.md](05-Workflow-Trigger-Events.md) (second half)
**Overview:**

- Advanced filtering: paths, branches, tags
- Webhook event patterns (push, pull request, release)
- Workflow_dispatch input types: string, choice, boolean, environment
- Default input values and required inputs
- Passing inputs to reusable workflows

**Practical Work:**

- Create workflows with branch and path filtering
- Implement sophisticated workflow_dispatch input validation
- Pass inputs from parent to reusable workflows
- Test trigger behavior with different event types


<details>
<summary>🏷️ Scenario 6-A: Advanced `paths-ignore` and Tag-Based Triggers</summary>


**Objective:** Combine `paths-ignore`, tag triggers, and `workflow_run` to build a layered trigger strategy that fires at the right times without redundant runs.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–5 complete; understanding of branches and tags in Git.

**Context:** Section 5 covered basic path filtering. Section 6 extends this with `paths-ignore` (the inverse approach), tag-based release triggers, and the `workflow_run` event that chains workflows together — a critical pattern for post-build deployment pipelines.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/release-pipeline.yml`:

```yaml
name: Release Pipeline

on:
  push:
    # Trigger on semantic version tags only
    tags:
      - 'v[0-9]+.[0-9]+.[0-9]+'    # matches v1.0.0, v2.3.14
      - 'v[0-9]+.[0-9]+.[0-9]+-rc*' # matches v1.0.0-rc1

  # Trigger when the 'CI' workflow completes successfully on main
  workflow_run:
    workflows: ['CI']
    branches: [main]
    types: [completed]

jobs:
  release:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    # Only proceed if triggered by tag OR if the upstream workflow succeeded
    if: |
      github.event_name == 'push' ||
      (github.event_name == 'workflow_run' && github.event.workflow_run.conclusion == 'success')
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Extract version from tag
        if: github.event_name == 'push'
        run: |
          TAG="${{ github.ref_name }}"
          echo "Releasing version: $TAG"
          echo "VERSION=$TAG" >> $GITHUB_ENV

      - name: Release triggered by workflow_run
        if: github.event_name == 'workflow_run'
        run: |
          echo "Triggered by workflow: ${{ github.event.workflow_run.name }}"
          echo "Source commit: ${{ github.event.workflow_run.head_sha }}"
```

2. Create a test workflow `.github/workflows/ci.yml` (referenced by `workflow_run`):

```yaml
name: CI

on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
    paths-ignore:
      - '**.md'          # ignore all markdown files anywhere
      - 'docs/**'        # ignore entire docs directory
      - '.github/CODEOWNERS'

jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: echo "CI tests passed"
```

3. Push a commit touching only `README.md` → verify CI workflow is **not** triggered.
4. Push a commit touching `src/main.js` → verify CI triggers, which then triggers the Release Pipeline.
5. Create and push a tag `v1.0.0` → verify Release Pipeline triggers directly from the tag.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] `paths-ignore: ['**.md']` prevents CI from running on markdown-only changes.
- [ ] `workflow_run` triggers Release Pipeline after CI succeeds on `main`.
- [ ] Tag push `v1.0.0` triggers Release Pipeline with `github.ref_name = v1.0.0`.
- [ ] `workflow_run` conditional correctly skips the release if CI failed.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`workflow_run` not triggering** → The triggering workflow (`CI`) must exist on the default branch at the time the `workflow_run` listener is evaluated.
- **Tag pattern not matching** → Test regex patterns at [crontab.guru](https://crontab.guru) or GitHub's filter pattern documentation. Remember `*` in YAML glob patterns requires quoting if the line starts with `*`.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>📤 Scenario 6-B: Pass `workflow_dispatch` Inputs to a Reusable Workflow</summary>


**Objective:** Create a caller workflow that accepts `workflow_dispatch` inputs and forwards them to a reusable `workflow_call` workflow without redefining each input twice.

**Prerequisites:** Section 5-B (workflow_dispatch inputs); Section 17 preview is helpful but not required.

**Context:** A common enterprise pattern is a top-level "deployment orchestrator" that collects user input via `workflow_dispatch` and delegates execution to standardized reusable workflows. Understanding how inputs flow from `workflow_dispatch` → caller → callee is a GH-200 exam topic.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create the reusable workflow `.github/workflows/deploy-reusable.yml`:

```yaml
name: Deploy Reusable

on:
  workflow_call:
    inputs:
      environment:
        required: true
        type: string
      version:
        required: true
        type: string
      dry-run:
        required: false
        type: boolean
        default: true
    secrets:
      DEPLOY_TOKEN:
        required: false

jobs:
  deploy:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Execute deployment
        run: |
          echo "=== Reusable Workflow Received ==="
          echo "Environment : ${{ inputs.environment }}"
          echo "Version     : ${{ inputs.version }}"
          echo "Dry run     : ${{ inputs.dry-run }}"
          echo "Token set   : ${{ secrets.DEPLOY_TOKEN != '' }}"
```

2. Create the caller workflow `.github/workflows/deploy-orchestrator.yml`:

```yaml
name: Deploy Orchestrator

on:
  workflow_dispatch:
    inputs:
      environment:
        description: 'Target environment'
        required: true
        type: choice
        options: [development, staging, production]
      version:
        description: 'Version to deploy'
        required: true
        type: string
      dry-run:
        description: 'Dry run mode'
        type: boolean
        default: true

jobs:
  orchestrate:
    # Pass workflow_dispatch inputs directly to the reusable workflow
    uses: ./.github/workflows/deploy-reusable.yml
    with:
      # Map dispatch inputs to reusable workflow inputs
      environment: ${{ inputs.environment }}
      version: ${{ inputs.version }}
      dry-run: ${{ inputs.dry-run }}
    secrets:
      DEPLOY_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.DEPLOY_TOKEN }}
```

3. Trigger the orchestrator via `workflow_dispatch` with `environment: staging`, `version: 1.2.3`.
4. Verify the reusable workflow job receives and prints all three values correctly.
5. Confirm the jobs appear under the `Deploy Orchestrator` workflow run, not as a separate run.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Caller workflow `jobs` block uses `uses:` with a `.yml` path (not `run:`).
- [ ] `with:` block correctly maps `inputs.*` to the reusable workflow's declared inputs.
- [ ] Secret is forwarded with `secrets:` block (not `with:`).
- [ ] Reusable workflow job is shown as a nested job under the caller's run.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 17 (Reusable Workflows) for full coverage of `workflow_call` including outputs and versioning; → Section 8 (Environments) for adding required reviewer gates to the `deploy` job inside the reusable workflow.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>📨 Scenario 6-C: Advanced Repository Dispatch — Custom Event Types and Complex Payloads</summary>


**Objective:** Master `repository_dispatch` event type for triggering workflows programmatically with rich payloads, enabling CI orchestration from scripts or external systems.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 6-B complete; familiarity with JSON payloads.

**Context:** `repository_dispatch` enables external systems (monitoring tools, deployment platforms, scripts) to trigger GitHub Actions workflows with custom data, bridging GitHub with enterprise tools.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/repository-dispatch-listener.yml`:

```yaml
name: Repository Dispatch Listener

on:
  repository_dispatch:
    types:
      - 'custom-build'
      - 'integration-test'
      - 'rollback'

jobs:
  handle-dispatch:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Log event details
        run: |
          echo "Event: ${{ github.event_name }}"
          echo "Type: ${{ github.event.action }}"
          echo "Payload: ${{ toJSON(github.event.client_payload) }}"

      - name: Route by event type
        run: |
          case "${{ github.event.action }}" in
            custom-build)
              echo "Building: ${{ github.event.client_payload.build-target }}"
              ;;
            integration-test)
              echo "Testing: ${{ github.event.client_payload.test-suite }}"
              ;;
            rollback)
              echo "Rolling back: ${{ github.event.client_payload.previous-version }}"
              ;;
          esac
```

2. Trigger via curl with custom payload:

```bash
curl -X POST \
  -H "Authorization: token ${{ secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN }}" \
  -H "Accept: application/vnd.github.v3+json" \
  https://api.github.com/repos/YOUR_ORG/YOUR_REPO/dispatches \
  -d '{
    "event_type": "custom-build",
    "client_payload": {
      "build-target": "production",
      "rebuild-cache": false
    }
  }'
```

3. Test event routing and payload access.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Repository dispatch events trigger workflow correctly
- [ ] Multiple event types are routed to correct handlers
- [ ] Client payload is accessible and properly parsed
- [ ] External curl request triggers workflow successfully

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 5 (Trigger Events Part 1) for basic trigger concepts; → Section 16 (REST API) for advanced API integration.

</details>

<details>
<summary>🐛 Scenario 6-D: Debugging Event Trigger Failures in Monorepo — Path-Based Workflow Selection</summary>


**Objective:** Diagnose and fix workflows in monorepo structures where path changes should trigger specific workflows but fail silently.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 6-C complete; understanding of monorepo layouts.

**Context:** In monorepo projects with multiple services (frontend/, backend/, infra/), different services require different CI workflows. Incorrect path filters cause workflows to skip unexpectedly.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create monorepo structure:

```bash
/frontend
  /.github/workflows/frontend-tests.yml
/backend
  /.github/workflows/backend-tests.yml
/shared
  /components
```

2. Create `.github/workflows/frontend-tests.yml`:

```yaml
name: Frontend Tests
on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
    paths:
      - 'frontend/**'
      - 'shared/**'
      - '.github/workflows/frontend-tests.yml'
jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - run: npm test --prefix frontend
```

3. Create `.github/workflows/backend-tests.yml`:

```yaml
name: Backend Tests
on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
    paths:
      - 'backend/**'
      - 'shared/**'
      - '.github/workflows/backend-tests.yml'
jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - run: npm test --prefix backend
```

4. Test scenarios:
   - Push only backend/ changes → Backend Tests runs, Frontend Tests skipped ✓
   - Push only shared/ changes → Both run ✓
   - Push .md files only → Both skipped ✓

5. Debug failures using `git diff`:

```bash
git diff HEAD~1 --name-only | grep -E "^frontend/" && echo "Frontend trigger"
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Path filters correctly trigger only affected services
- [ ] Shared path changes trigger all affected workflows
- [ ] Non-code changes skip all workflows
- [ ] Can manually verify which files changed using git diff

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 5 (Trigger Events) for path filter syntax; → Section 28 (Practice Assessment) for real monorepo scenarios.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🏢 Scenario 6-E: Enterprise Event Coordination — Multi-Trigger Gating and Approval Chains</summary>


**Objective:** Build sophisticated workflows combining multiple trigger types with approvals and conditional routing for enterprise-safe deployments.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 6-D complete; understanding of environment protection.

**Context:** Enterprises need multi-step gates: PR code review + approval + scheduled security scan before production deployment. This scenario chains triggers with manual gates.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/enterprise-deployment-gate.yml`:

```yaml
name: Enterprise Deployment Gate

on:
  workflow_dispatch:
    inputs:
      target_env:
        description: 'Deployment target'
        type: choice
        options: [staging, production]
      skip_tests:
        description: 'Skip test suite (requires approval)'
        type: boolean
        default: false
  schedule:
    - cron: '0 2 * * 1'  # Monday 2am - production health check
  push:
    branches: [main]
    paths:
      - 'src/**'
      - 'infra/**'

jobs:
  determine-action:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      should-deploy: ${{ steps.logic.outputs.deploy }}
      target: ${{ steps.logic.outputs.target }}
    steps:
      - name: Routing logic
        id: logic
        run: |
          if [ "${{ github.event_name }}" = "schedule" ]; then
            # Scheduled: prod health check only
            echo "deploy=true" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
            echo "target=production" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          elif [ "${{ github.event_name }}" = "workflow_dispatch" ]; then
            # Manual dispatch: use input
            echo "deploy=true" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
            echo "target=${{ inputs.target_env }}" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          else
            # Push: staging only (needs approval for prod)
            echo "deploy=true" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
            echo "target=staging" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          fi

  test:
    needs: determine-action
    if: needs.determine-action.outputs.should-deploy == 'true' && !inputs.skip_tests
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - run: npm test

  security-scan:
    needs: determine-action
    if: needs.determine-action.outputs.target == 'production'
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - run: echo "Running security scan..."

  approval-gate:
    needs: [determine-action, test, security-scan]
    if: needs.determine-action.outputs.target == 'production' && github.event_name == 'push'
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment:
      name: prod-approval-required
    steps:
      - run: echo "⏳ Awaiting production approval..."

  deploy:
    needs: [determine-action, approval-gate]
    if: always() && needs.determine-action.outputs.should-deploy == 'true'
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment:
      name: ${{ needs.determine-action.outputs.target }}
      url: https://${{ needs.determine-action.outputs.target }}.myapp.com
    steps:
      - name: Deploy to ${{ needs.determine-action.outputs.target }}
        run: echo "🚀 Deploying to ${{ needs.determine-action.outputs.target }}"

  notify:
    if: always()
    needs: deploy
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: echo "Deployment status: ${{ needs.deploy.result }}"
```

2. Test each trigger path:
   - Manual dispatch to staging → skips approval
   - Manual dispatch to production → requires approval
   - Monday 2am → health check only
   - Push to main → stages to staging, waits for approval if promoting to prod

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Workflow routes correctly based on trigger type
- [ ] Scheduled runs skip approval gates
- [ ] Manual dispatch to production requires environment approval
- [ ] Test suite can be skipped (with trace audit)
- [ ] All paths eventually deploy to correct environment

**Time Estimate:** 30–40 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 8 (Environment Protection) for approval gate setup; → Section 3 (Conditional Logic) for complex if: expressions.

</details>

### ⚙️ Section 7: Environment & Runtime Configuration (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [06-Custom-Environment-Variables.md](06-Custom-Environment-Variables.md), [07-Default-Environment-Variables.md](07-Default-Environment-Variables.md)
**Overview:**

- Custom environment variables at workflow, job, and step levels
- Default environment variables (GITHUB_*, CI, RUNNER_*, etc.)
- Setting variables dynamically with GITHUB_ENV
- Variable scoping and precedence rules
- Secret-safe variable handling
- GitHub Actions context interpolation in env vars

**Practical Work:**

- Define custom environment variables at workflow scope
- Set runtime environment variables in steps
- Access default environment variables in scripts
- Implement environment-based configuration (dev/prod)
- Verify variable scope and precedence


<details>
<summary>⚙️ Scenario 7-A: Environment Variable Scope and Precedence</summary>


**Objective:** Demonstrate that step-level `env:` overrides job-level, which overrides workflow-level, and verify the exact precedence order in logs.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–4 complete; understanding of YAML structure.

**Context:** Environment variable scoping is a common source of confusion and a confirmed GH-200 exam topic. Variables at different levels serve different purposes: workflow-level sets defaults, job-level configures a specific job context, step-level enables targeted overrides without affecting sibling steps.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/env-precedence.yml`:

```yaml
name: Env Var Precedence

on: workflow_dispatch

# Workflow-level env: available to all jobs and steps
env:
  APP_ENV: workflow-level
  SHARED_VAR: from-workflow

jobs:
  precedence-demo:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest

    # Job-level env: overrides workflow-level for this job
    env:
      APP_ENV: job-level  # overrides workflow-level APP_ENV

    steps:
      - name: Step 1 — inherits job-level APP_ENV
        run: |
          echo "APP_ENV    = $APP_ENV"       # expects: job-level
          echo "SHARED_VAR = $SHARED_VAR"    # expects: from-workflow

      - name: Step 2 — step-level override
        env:
          APP_ENV: step-level  # overrides job-level APP_ENV for this step only
        run: |
          echo "APP_ENV    = $APP_ENV"       # expects: step-level
          echo "SHARED_VAR = $SHARED_VAR"    # expects: from-workflow (unchanged)

      - name: Step 3 — back to job-level after step 2
        run: |
          echo "APP_ENV    = $APP_ENV"       # expects: job-level (step override gone)
```

2. Trigger via `workflow_dispatch` and verify each step's output matches expectations.
3. Add `APP_ENV` as a **Repository Variable** (Settings → Secrets and variables → Variables) and observe it does NOT override the YAML-defined env vars (YAML env wins over repository variables that are set via `vars.` context, not plain env vars).

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Step 1 prints `APP_ENV = job-level`.
- [ ] Step 2 prints `APP_ENV = step-level`.
- [ ] Step 3 prints `APP_ENV = job-level` (step override scoped to Step 2 only).
- [ ] `SHARED_VAR` is `from-workflow` in all three steps.

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔄 Scenario 7-B: Dynamic Variables with `GITHUB_ENV` and Default Environment Variables</summary>


**Objective:** Use `$GITHUB_ENV` to set environment variables dynamically in one step and consume them in subsequent steps, and catalog the most important default GitHub-provided environment variables.

**Prerequisites:** Section 7-A complete.

**Context:** `$GITHUB_ENV` is the runtime mechanism for passing computed values between steps without using step outputs. It is commonly used to set a version number, a computed path, or a build timestamp that later steps need. Default environment variables like `GITHUB_WORKSPACE`, `GITHUB_SHA`, and `CI` are always available and their values are exam-tested.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/github-env-demo.yml`:

```yaml
name: GITHUB_ENV Demo

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  env-demo:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Step 1 — Compute and set dynamic variables
        run: |
          # Set multiple variables by appending to $GITHUB_ENV
          echo "BUILD_DATE=$(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ)" >> $GITHUB_ENV
          echo "SHORT_SHA=${GITHUB_SHA:0:7}" >> $GITHUB_ENV
          echo "BUILD_TAG=build-${{ github.run_number }}" >> $GITHUB_ENV

      - name: Step 2 — Use variables set in Step 1
        run: |
          echo "Build date : $BUILD_DATE"
          echo "Short SHA  : $SHORT_SHA"
          echo "Build tag  : $BUILD_TAG"

      - name: Step 3 — Default GitHub-provided environment variables
        run: |
          echo "=== Runner Info ==="
          echo "CI              : $CI"
          echo "RUNNER_OS       : $RUNNER_OS"
          echo "RUNNER_ARCH     : $RUNNER_ARCH"
          echo "RUNNER_TEMP     : $RUNNER_TEMP"

          echo "=== Repository Info ==="
          echo "GITHUB_REPOSITORY      : $GITHUB_REPOSITORY"
          echo "GITHUB_SHA             : $GITHUB_SHA"
          echo "GITHUB_REF             : $GITHUB_REF"
          echo "GITHUB_REF_NAME        : $GITHUB_REF_NAME"
          echo "GITHUB_WORKSPACE       : $GITHUB_WORKSPACE"
          echo "GITHUB_RUN_ID          : $GITHUB_RUN_ID"
          echo "GITHUB_RUN_NUMBER      : $GITHUB_RUN_NUMBER"
          echo "GITHUB_ACTIONS         : $GITHUB_ACTIONS"
```

2. Trigger and verify Step 2 uses the values set in Step 1 (not empty).
3. Note that `GITHUB_ENV` values are **not** available in the same step that sets them — only in subsequent steps.
4. Verify `$CI` = `true` and `$GITHUB_ACTIONS` = `true` — useful for branching logic in scripts.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] `BUILD_DATE` in Step 2 shows a UTC ISO timestamp.
- [ ] `SHORT_SHA` is a 7-character hex string matching the first 7 chars of `GITHUB_SHA`.
- [ ] `RUNNER_OS` shows `Linux` on `ubuntu-latest` runners.
- [ ] `GITHUB_WORKSPACE` shows the path where the repository is checked out.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`BUILD_DATE` is empty in Step 2** → Variable set in Step 1 via `>> $GITHUB_ENV` is only available in *subsequent* steps. If you read it in the same step, it will be empty.
- **`GITHUB_ENV` not found** → This is a file path, not an env var itself. Use `>> $GITHUB_ENV` (append to file); do not `export` the variable directly.

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Variations/Extensions:**

- Use multiline value syntax: `echo "MULTILINE_VAR<<EOF" >> $GITHUB_ENV && echo "line1" >> $GITHUB_ENV && echo "EOF" >> $GITHUB_ENV`
- Compare `GITHUB_ENV` (sets env vars for later steps) with `GITHUB_OUTPUT` (sets step outputs for other jobs).

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 8 (Environments) for environment-specific variables (not the same as `env:`); → Section 14 (Inter-Job Communication) for `GITHUB_OUTPUT` patterns.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🖥️ Scenario 7-C: Platform-Specific Paths and Cross-Platform Configuration</summary>


**Objective:** Handle environment variable differences across platforms (Linux, macOS, Windows) for truly portable workflows.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 7-B complete; understanding of runner platforms.

**Context:** Professional workflows must run on multiple runners (ubuntu-latest, macos-latest, windows-latest). Path separators, shell differences, and environment variables differ per platform.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/cross-platform-env.yml`:

```yaml
name: Cross-Platform Environment

on: [push]

jobs:
  env-vars:
    strategy:
      matrix:
        os: [ubuntu-latest, windows-latest, macos-latest]
    runs-on: ${{ matrix.os }}
    defaults:
      run:
        shell: bash  # bash on all platforms (even Windows)
    steps:
      - name: Print platform-specific vars
        run: |
          echo "OS: ${{ runner.os }}"
          echo "Path separator: $([ "${{ runner.os }}" = "Windows" ] && echo "\\" || echo "/")"
          echo "Shell: $SHELL"
          echo "Temp dir: $RUNNER_TEMP"

      - name: Set platform-specific paths
        run: |
          if [ "${{ runner.os }}" = "Windows" ]; then
            echo "ARTIFACT_PATH=$(pwd)\\dist" >> $GITHUB_ENV
          else
            echo "ARTIFACT_PATH=$(pwd)/dist" >> $GITHUB_ENV
          fi

      - name: Use platform-aware paths
        run: mkdir -p "$ARTIFACT_PATH"
```

2. Run on all platforms; verify paths are correct for each.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Runner OS correctly detected for each platform
- [ ] Path separators handled correctly
- [ ] Shell defaults to bash on Windows
- [ ] Artifact paths are valid for each platform

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 20 (GitHub-Hosted Runners) for runner environment details; → Section 15 (Debugging) for cross-platform troubleshooting.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔧 Scenario 7-D: Fix Environment Variable Pollution — Isolating Variables Between Jobs</summary>


**Objective:** Diagnose and fix scenarios where environment variables leak between jobs or persist unexpectedly.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 7-C complete; understanding of job isolation.

**Context:** Environment variables are job-scoped but some values unintentionally leak via GITHUB_ENV or global settings, causing confusing failures.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/env-pollution-debug.yml`:

```yaml
name: Environment Pollution Debug

on: workflow_dispatch

env:
  GLOBAL_VAR: "global"

jobs:
  job1:
    env:
      JOB1_VAR: "from-job1"
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Set via GITHUB_ENV
        run: echo "LEAKED_VAR=should-not-leak" >> $GITHUB_ENV

  job2:
    needs: job1
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Try to access job1 var
        run: |
          echo "LEAKED_VAR: ${LEAKED_VAR:-NOT_FOUND}"
          echo "JOB1_VAR: ${JOB1_VAR:-NOT_FOUND}"
          echo "GLOBAL_VAR: $GLOBAL_VAR"
          # Job2 should see GLOBAL_VAR but NOT LEAKED_VAR or JOB1_VAR
```

2. Run and verify: job2 has access ONLY to GLOBAL_VAR.
3. Fix: Use job outputs instead of GITHUB_ENV for inter-job communication.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Understand job-level isolation
- [ ] Know that GITHUB_ENV is job-scoped, not global
- [ ] Can use outputs for safe inter-job communication

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 14 (Inter-Job Communication) for outputs; → Section 2 (Contexts) for context scope rules.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🏢 Scenario 7-E: Enterprise Environment Configuration — Shared Org Settings and Team Defaults</summary>


**Objective:** Build reusable, organization-wide environment variable configurations deployed across all workflows.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 7-D complete; understanding of reusable workflows.

**Context:** Large organizations want consistent environment variables (NODE_VERSION, CACHE_PREFIX, etc.) across all repos without copying. This scenario teaches centralized defaults.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create a reusable "env setup" workflow at `org/.github/workflows/reusable-env-setup.yml`:

```yaml
name: Reusable Env Setup

on:
  workflow_call:
    inputs:
      environment:
        type: string
        default: 'staging'

jobs:
  setup:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      node-version: '20'
      npm-cache: 'npm-cache-${{ github.sha }}'
    steps:
      - name: Setup org defaults
        run: |
          echo "Org Defaults:"
          echo "  Node: 20"
          echo "  Cache: npm-cache-${{ github.sha }}"
          echo "  Env: ${{ inputs.environment }}"
```

2. Use in main workflows:

```yaml
name: Main Workflow

on: push

jobs:
  setup:
    uses: .org/.github/workflows/reusable-env-setup.yml
    with:
      environment: production

  build:
    needs: setup
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    env:
      NODE_VERSION: ${{ needs.setup.outputs.node-version }}
    steps:
      - uses: actions/setup-node@v4
        with:
          node-version: ${{ env.NODE_VERSION }}
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Reusable workflow defines org-standard env vars
- [ ] Outputs from reusable workflow are used in dependent jobs
- [ ] Environment-specific values switch based on input
- [ ] Changes to reusable workflow affect all consumers

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 17 (Reusable Workflows) for workflow composition; → Section 22 (Enterprise Management) for org-wide policies.

</details>

### 🔐 Section 8: Environment Protection Rules & Secrets (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [08-Environment-Protection-Rules.md](08-Environment-Protection-Rules.md)
**Overview:**

- Environment concepts and deployment targets
- Environment-specific secrets and variables
- Protection rules: required reviewers, deployment branches
- Manual approval gates in workflows
- GITHUB_TOKEN permissions and scoping
- PAT (Personal Access Token) vs GITHUB_TOKEN lifecycle

**Practical Work:**

- Create environments with different configurations
- Set up environment-specific secrets
- Implement required reviewer protection rules
- Configure deployment branch restrictions
- Test approval workflows


<details>
<summary>🛡️ Scenario 8-A: Create Environments with Protection Rules and Secrets</summary>


**Objective:** Set up three GitHub Environments (`development`, `staging`, `production`) with escalating protection, environment-specific secrets, and branch deployment restrictions.

**Prerequisites:** Repository admin access; Sections 1–7 complete; a GitHub repository (personal or organization).

**Context:** GitHub Environments are named deployment targets that carry their own secrets, variables, and protection rules. They are referenced in workflow `jobs` via the `environment:` key. The `production` environment commonly requires manual approval before a deploy job runs — a gate that cannot be bypassed by pushing code.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Navigate to **Repository → Settings → Environments → New environment**.
2. Create three environments with these settings:

   | Environment | Deployment branch rule | Required reviewers |
   |---|---|---|
   | `development` | Any branch | None |
   | `staging` | Selected branches: `main` | None |
   | `production` | Selected branches: `main`, `release/**` | Add yourself as reviewer |

3. For each environment, add an environment-specific secret:
   - `development`: `DATABASE_URL = postgres://dev-host:5432/devdb`
   - `staging`: `DATABASE_URL = postgres://stg-host:5432/stgdb`
   - `production`: `DATABASE_URL = postgres://prod-host:5432/proddb`

4. Create `.github/workflows/multi-env-deploy.yml`:

```yaml
name: Multi-Environment Deploy

on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
  workflow_dispatch:
    inputs:
      target-env:
        description: 'Target environment'
        type: choice
        options: [development, staging, production]
        default: development

jobs:
  deploy-dev:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment: development  # links job to the 'development' environment
    steps:
      - name: Deploy to development
        env:
          DB_URL: ${{ secrets.DATABASE_URL }}  # resolves from development environment
        run: |
          echo "Deploying to development"
          echo "DB URL length: ${#DB_URL}"  # print length, not value

  deploy-staging:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: deploy-dev
    environment: staging
    steps:
      - name: Deploy to staging
        env:
          DB_URL: ${{ secrets.DATABASE_URL }}  # resolves from staging environment
        run: echo "Deployed to staging — DB: ${#DB_URL} chars"

  deploy-production:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: deploy-staging
    # Named environment with URL shown in the Actions UI
    environment:
      name: production
      url: https://myapp.example.com
    steps:
      - name: Deploy to production
        env:
          DB_URL: ${{ secrets.DATABASE_URL }}  # resolves from production environment
        run: echo "Deployed to production — DB: ${#DB_URL} chars"
```

5. Push to `main` and watch the workflow run.
6. When the `deploy-production` job appears, it should pause and show **"Waiting for review"**.
7. Click "Review pending deployments" → approve → verify the job runs.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Each job uses a different `DATABASE_URL` secret from its respective environment.
- [ ] `deploy-production` pauses for reviewer approval before executing.
- [ ] After approval, the production job runs and shows the environment URL.
- [ ] Rejecting the review marks the job as "Rejected" and skips execution.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Approval not appearing** → Verify the `production` environment has at least one required reviewer. The reviewer cannot be the same user who triggered the workflow (for org-level; personal repos allow self-review).
- **`DATABASE_URL` is empty** → Confirm the secret is added to the **environment** (not the repository). Environment secrets are separate from repository secrets.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔑 Scenario 8-B: GITHUB_TOKEN Permissions and Scoping</summary>


**Objective:** Understand the default `GITHUB_TOKEN` permissions, restrict them using the `permissions:` key, and observe the difference between repo-level default and job-level explicit scoping.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 8-A complete; understanding of GitHub API scopes.

**Context:** `GITHUB_TOKEN` is automatically provisioned for every workflow run and expires when the run completes. Its default permissions vary by repository settings (permissive vs restrictive default). For security, workflows should declare only the permissions they need — a requirement that some organizations enforce via policy.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/token-permissions.yml`:

```yaml
name: GITHUB_TOKEN Permissions

on: workflow_dispatch

# Restrict workflow-level permissions to read-only baseline
permissions:
  contents: read
  metadata: read

jobs:
  read-only:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    # Job inherits workflow-level permissions unless overridden
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Attempt to read repo (should succeed)
        run: |
          echo "SHA: ${{ github.sha }}"
          git log --oneline -3

  write-issues:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    # Job-level permissions override workflow-level for this job only
    permissions:
      issues: write
      contents: read
    steps:
      - name: Create a test issue comment
        uses: actions/github-script@v7
        with:
          script: |
            // github.token automatically uses the job's scoped GITHUB_TOKEN
            await github.rest.issues.createComment({
              owner: context.repo.owner,
              repo: context.repo.repo,
              issue_number: 1,  // assumes issue #1 exists
              body: `Test comment from workflow run #${context.runNumber}`
            })

  no-write-attempt:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    permissions:
      contents: read  # intentionally no 'issues: write'
    steps:
      - name: This step cannot write issues
        run: |
          # Using curl to demonstrate the 403 you would get
          echo "Token permissions: contents=read only"
          echo "Any write operation to issues would return 403"
```

2. Trigger via `workflow_dispatch`.
3. Verify the `write-issues` job creates a comment on Issue #1.
4. Note that `no-write-attempt` acknowledges the limitation (for exam understanding).
5. Navigate to the run details and click the token information icon to see granted permissions.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] `write-issues` job successfully posts a comment (proving `issues: write` scope works).
- [ ] `read-only` job can read repo content but has no write capabilities.
- [ ] Workflow-level `permissions:` restricts both jobs unless overridden.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Issue comment creation fails with 403** → Check that Issue #1 exists, or change the issue number. Also verify `issues: write` is in the `write-issues` job's `permissions:` block.
- **`permissions:` key not recognized** → Only available at workflow-level and job-level; not at step-level.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 23 (Security) for principle of least privilege applied to GITHUB_TOKEN; → Section 24 (Security Part 2) for OIDC as an alternative to long-lived tokens.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔐 Scenario 8-C: OpenID Connect (OIDC) Token Generation and Short-Lived Credential Exchange</summary>


**Objective:** Replace long-lived personal access tokens with short-lived OIDC tokens for enhanced security in cloud provider authentication.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 8-B complete; AWS/Azure account setup (or understanding of cloud provider OIDC).

**Context:** Long-lived credentials are security risks. OIDC enables GitHub to issue short-lived tokens that expire within 15 minutes, significantly reducing blast radius of credential compromise.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/oidc-aws-auth.yml` (AWS example):

```yaml
name: OIDC AWS Authentication

on: [push]

permissions:
  id-token: write  # Allow OIDC token generation

jobs:
  deploy-to-aws:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Generate OIDC token
        id: oidc
        uses: aws-actions/configure-aws-credentials@v4
        with:
          role-to-assume: arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/GitHubActionsRole
          aws-region: us-east-1

      - name: List S3 buckets (via OIDC auth)
        run: aws s3 ls

      - name: View token expiry (check in browser CloudTrail)
        run: |
          echo "Token expires in ~15 minutes"
          echo "No long-lived credentials stored"
```

2. Configure AWS IAM role to trust GitHub:
   - Create trust policy accepting OIDC issuer: `token.actions.githubusercontent.com`
   - Scope to specific org/repo combinations

3. Compare token lifetime: OIDC (15 min) vs PAT (never expires).

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] OIDC token successfully authenticates to AWS
- [ ] No PAT/credentials stored in GitHub secrets
- [ ] AWS CloudTrail shows temporary role assumption
- [ ] Token is automatically renewed per job if job runs longer

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 16 (REST API) for programmatic credential handling; → Section 23 (Security) for secure credential patterns.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔍 Scenario 8-D: Audit and Recover from Excessive GITHUB_TOKEN Permissions</summary>


**Objective:** Identify workflows with over-permissioned tokens and remediate security risks through permission auditing.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 8-C complete; exposure to GitHub security alerts.

**Context:** Workflows sometimes grant `contents: write` when only `contents: read` is needed. Over-permissioned tokens increase risk if compromised. Learning to audit and correct improves security posture.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/audit-permissions.yml` (intentionally over-permissioned):

```yaml
name: Audit Permissions

on: push

permissions:
  contents: write  # Should probably be 'read'
  issues: write    # Not used in this workflow
  pull-requests: write  # Overkill

jobs:
  audit:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Run linter (read-only operation)
        run: echo "Linting code..."
        # ^ This only needs 'contents: read', not 'write'

      - name: Print actual scope needed
        run: |
          echo "This workflow only reads repo content."
          echo "Scope should be: permissions: { contents: read }"
```

2. Audit using GitHub's built-in security scans:
   - Go to Repo → **Security → Secret scanning → Code scanning**
   - Look for warnings about over-permissioned workflows

3. Refactor workflow to least privilege:

```yaml
permissions:
  contents: read  # Minimal required
```

4. Verify GitHub Actions still work with reduced permissions.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Identify unnecessary permissions in workflow
- [ ] Reduce permissions to minimum required
- [ ] Workflow still succeeds with reduced permissions
- [ ] GitHub security scanner no longer flags the workflow

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 23 (Security) for least-privilege principles; → Section 2 (Contexts) for available permission scopes.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔄 Scenario 8-E: Organization-Wide Secret Rotation and Secure Cross-Repository Access</summary>


**Objective:** Implement secrets management across an organization: centralizing secret provisioning, enforcing rotation policies, and enabling safe cross-repo access.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 8-D complete; organization admin access.

**Context:** Managing secrets at scale requires central control: versioning, rotation schedules, and audit trails. This scenario teaches enterprise-grade secrets architecture.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. **Create Organization Secret (Org Admin):**
   - Go to Org **Settings → Secrets and variables → Actions**
   - Create `ORG_DATABASE_URL = postgresql://prod.db.internal`
   - Set **Repository access** to "All repositories"

2. **Create Rotation Automation:**
   - Create `.github/workflows/rotate-org-secrets.yml`:

     ```yaml
     name: Rotate Org Secrets
     on:
       schedule:
         - cron: '0 0 1 * *'  # Monthly
     jobs:
       rotate:
         runs-on: ubuntu-latest
         steps:
           - name: Generate new secret
             id: gen
             run: |
               NEW_VALUE=$(openssl rand -base64 32 | tr -d "=+/" | cut -c1-25)
               echo "new_secret=$NEW_VALUE" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

           - name: Update via GitHub API
             env:
               GH_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.ROTATION_TOKEN }}
             run: |
               gh secret set ORG_DATABASE_URL -b "${NEW_VALUE}" --org YOUR_ORG
     ```

3. **Enable Across Organization:**
   - Repository-level workflows inherit org secret: `${{ secrets.ORG_DATABASE_URL }}`
   - All repos see rotated value automatically

4. **Audit Via API:**
   - Query secret access logs via GitHub GraphQL
   - Track which workflows accessed which secrets

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Organization secret is defined and available to member repos
- [ ] Monthly rotation workflow updates secret value
- [ ] All repos pick up new value without individual updates
- [ ] Rotation audit trail is maintained

**Time Estimate:** 30–40 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 2 (Contexts) for secret masking; → Section 22 (Enterprise Management) for org-wide policies.

</details>

## 🔄 Week 3: Workflow Features & Data Flow

### 🔀 Section 9: Jobs, Steps & Conditional Logic (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [04-Workflow-File-Structure.md](04-Workflow-File-Structure.md) (jobs section)
**Overview:**

- Job definition and identification
- Step structure: name, id, uses, run, with, env
- Sequential vs parallel job execution
- Job dependencies with `needs`
- Conditional execution: if expressions with success/failure functions
- Failure handling: continue-on-error, failure markers

**Practical Work:**

- Create multi-job workflows with dependencies
- Implement conditional steps using if expressions
- Use success(), failure(), and cancelled() functions
- Handle errors and continue execution strategically
- Test job ordering and failure scenarios


<details>
<summary>🔗 Scenario 9-A: Multi-Job Dependencies with Status Functions</summary>


**Objective:** Build a 4-job pipeline where jobs run in a specific order, one deliberately fails, and downstream jobs use `failure()`, `success()`, and `always()` to respond appropriately.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–8 complete; basic understanding of job dependencies from Sections 3 and 4.

**Context:** Real pipelines require more nuanced dependency logic than simple sequential chaining. A `notify` job should run even when `deploy` fails. A `cleanup` job should always run. Understanding when `success()`, `failure()`, `cancelled()`, and `always()` evaluate is a core GH-200 exam topic.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/job-conditions.yml`:

```yaml
name: Job Conditions Demo

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Build application
        run: echo "Build complete — artifact ready"

  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: build
    steps:
      - name: Run tests
        # Force a test failure to demonstrate downstream behavior
        run: |
          echo "Running tests..."
          exit 1  # simulate test failure

  deploy:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: test
    # Default: only runs if 'test' succeeded — will be SKIPPED after test fails
    steps:
      - run: echo "Deploying application"

  notify:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    # Runs after both test and deploy, regardless of their outcomes
    needs: [test, deploy]
    if: always()  # always() returns true even when upstream jobs failed/cancelled
    steps:
      - name: Notify on failure
        if: failure()  # failure() is true if any upstream job failed
        run: echo "❌ Pipeline FAILED — would send failure alert"

      - name: Notify on success
        if: success()  # success() is true only if all upstream jobs succeeded
        run: echo "✅ Pipeline SUCCEEDED — would send success notification"

      - name: Always log status
        # always() at the step level runs even if prior steps in this job failed
        if: always()
        run: |
          echo "Pipeline status captured"
          echo "Upstream conclusion available via needs context"
```

2. Trigger the workflow and observe the run graph:
   - `build` → succeeds
   - `test` → fails (exit 1)
   - `deploy` → skipped (depends on failed `test`)
   - `notify` → runs (due to `if: always()`)
3. In `notify`, verify the "Notify on failure" step runs and "Notify on success" is skipped.
4. Edit the workflow: remove `exit 1` from test, re-run, and verify all jobs succeed including the success notification.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] `deploy` shows as **Skipped** (not Failed) when `test` fails.
- [ ] `notify` shows as **Succeeded** despite upstream failures (due to `always()`).
- [ ] "Notify on failure" step runs when test failed; "Notify on success" is skipped.
- [ ] After fixing the test: all jobs succeed and "Notify on success" runs.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`notify` job is skipped instead of running** → The default `if:` condition is `success()`. Without `if: always()` on the job, it skips when any `needs` job failed. The `always()` must be on the **job**, not just a step.
- **`failure()` in step returns wrong value** → `failure()` at step level checks if any *previous step in the same job* failed, not upstream jobs. Use `needs.test.result == 'failure'` for upstream job status.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>⚠️ Scenario 9-B: `continue-on-error` and Step-Level Error Handling</summary>


**Objective:** Use `continue-on-error` to allow flaky steps to fail without failing the job, and use `outcome` step context to branch on step results.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 9-A complete.

**Context:** Some steps are inherently unreliable (external API calls, network checks) but should not block the workflow. `continue-on-error: true` allows the workflow to continue, and the `steps.<id>.outcome` and `steps.<id>.conclusion` values let subsequent steps react to what happened.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/continue-on-error.yml`:

```yaml
name: Continue on Error Demo

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  resilient-job:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Flaky external check
        id: flaky-check
        continue-on-error: true  # job continues even if this step fails
        run: |
          echo "Checking external service..."
          exit 1  # simulate flaky failure

      - name: React to flaky check result
        run: |
          echo "Flaky check outcome    : ${{ steps.flaky-check.outcome }}"
          echo "Flaky check conclusion : ${{ steps.flaky-check.conclusion }}"
          # outcome: success/failure/skipped/cancelled (the raw result)
          # conclusion: success/failure/skipped/cancelled (same unless continue-on-error overrides)

      - name: Only runs if flaky check passed
        if: steps.flaky-check.outcome == 'success'
        run: echo "External check OK — proceeding with dependent step"

      - name: Fallback when flaky check failed
        if: steps.flaky-check.outcome == 'failure'
        run: echo "External check unavailable — using fallback mode"

      - name: Final step — always runs, job is still green
        run: echo "Job completes successfully despite the failed flaky step"
```

2. Trigger and verify the job shows **green** even though a step exited with `exit 1`.
3. Observe the difference between `outcome` (raw) and `conclusion` (effective): with `continue-on-error: true`, `conclusion` is `success` but `outcome` is `failure`.
4. Change `continue-on-error: false` (or remove it) and verify the job now fails.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Job shows **green** (success) when `continue-on-error: true` and the step exits 1.
- [ ] `outcome` = `failure` and `conclusion` = `success` with `continue-on-error: true`.
- [ ] "Fallback" step runs; "Only runs if passed" step is skipped.
- [ ] After removing `continue-on-error`, the job turns **red**.

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 15 (Debugging) for using `outcome` context to build diagnostic workflows; → Section 26 (Troubleshooting) for matrix job failure scenarios.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>📊 Scenario 9-C: Advanced Job Dependencies with Outputs and Complex Routing</summary>


**Objective:** Build multi-job workflows with sophisticated dependency chains, passing data between jobs, and conditional routing based on outputs.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 9-B complete; understanding of job outputs.

**Context:** Enterprise workflows often require: job A produces output → job B uses that output → job C runs only if B succeeds. Mastering these patterns enables resilient automation.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/advanced-job-routing.yml`:

```yaml
name: Advanced Job Routing
on: [push]
jobs:
  analyze:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      should-deploy: ${{ steps.check.outputs.deploy }}
      version: ${{ steps.version.outputs.version }}
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - id: check
        run: echo "deploy=true" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
      - id: version
        run: echo "version=1.0.0" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

  build:
    needs: analyze
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      artifact-hash: abc123
    steps:
      - run: echo "Building v${{ needs.analyze.outputs.version }}"

  test:
    needs: build
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: echo "Testing artifact ${{ needs.build.outputs.artifact-hash }}"

  deploy:
    if: needs.analyze.outputs.should-deploy == 'true'
    needs: [analyze, test]
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: echo "Deploying v${{ needs.analyze.outputs.version }}"
```

2. Test: modify `should-deploy` output to `false` → deploy job skips.
3. Verify outputs pass through the dependency chain.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Job outputs available to dependent jobs via `needs.JOBID.outputs.KEY`
- [ ] Conditional job-level `if:` works with outputs
- [ ] Chain of 3+ jobs with multiple outputs passes data correctly

**Time Estimate:** 20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 14 (Outputs) for inter-job communication; → Section 3 (Conditionals) for complex if expressions.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>⏱️ Scenario 9-D: Timeout and Cancellation Handling</summary>


**Objective:** Diagnose and recover from job timeouts and workflow cancellations.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 9-C complete.

**Context:** Long-running jobs timeout (default 6 hours). Learning to handle graceful shutdown and detect cancellation prevents data loss.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create workflow with `timeout-minutes`:

```yaml
name: Timeout Demo
on: workflow_dispatch
jobs:
  long-job:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    timeout-minutes: 1
    steps:
      - name: Run slow task
        run: sleep 90  # Exceeds 1 min timeout
      - name: Never runs (job cancelled)
        run: echo "This is skipped"
```

2. Manually cancel workflow mid-run; observe `cancelled` status.
3. Add cleanup step that always runs:

```yaml
      - if: always()
        run: echo "Cleanup: timeout occurred at $(date)"
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Job shows `timed out` in UI when `timeout-minutes` exceeded
- [ ] `if: always()` cleanup step runs even on timeout/cancellation
- [ ] Subsequent jobs are skipped when upstream times out

**Time Estimate:** 15 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 15 (Debugging) for investigating timeouts; → Section 26 (Troubleshooting) for timeout recovery strategies.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔄 Scenario 9-E: Self-Healing Orchestration with Automatic Retry and Fallback Logic</summary>


**Objective:** Build resilient workflows that automatically retry failed jobs and activate fallback paths.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 9-D complete.

**Context:** Production workflows must handle transient failures. Implementing retry and fallback logic prevents false negatives and improves reliability.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/self-healing.yml`:

```yaml
name: Self-Healing Orchestration
on: [push]
jobs:
  flaky-operation:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Attempt (may fail randomly)
        run: |
          if [ $((RANDOM % 2)) -eq 0 ]; then
            exit 1
          fi
          echo "Success!"

  retry-handler:
    needs: flaky-operation
    if: failure()  # Runs if flaky-operation failed
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Log failure and retry
        run: echo "Retry via matrix or rerun button"

  fallback-path:
    needs: flaky-operation
    if: failure()
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Use cached result
        run: echo "Using fallback cached artifact"

  notify:
    if: always()
    needs: [flaky-operation, fallback-path]
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: |
          echo "Job status: ${{ needs.flaky-operation.result }}"
          echo "Fallback executed: ${{ needs.fallback-path.result != 'skipped' }}"
```

2. Observe: flaky job fails → fallback auto-activates.
3. On success: fallback skipped.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Fallback job activates on primary job failure
- [ ] Fallback is skipped if primary succeeds
- [ ] Notification always runs and reports status

**Time Estimate:** 20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 26 (Troubleshooting) for failure recovery; → Section 25 (Performance) for timeout tuning.

</details>

---

### 🔢 Section 10: Job Strategy & Matrix (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [04-Workflow-File-Structure.md](04-Workflow-File-Structure.md) (matrix section)
**Overview:**

- Strategy matrix for generating job variations
- Multi-axis matrix (OS, Python version, Node version combinations)
- Include/exclude matrix configurations
- Fail-fast behavior and max-parallel settings
- Matrix context access and interpolation
- Cost optimization with matrix configuration

**Practical Work:**

- Create multi-OS test matrices (Ubuntu, Windows, macOS)
- Build version compatibility matrices (Node, Python, Java)
- Use include/exclude for special variations
- Optimize matrix for cost and speed
- Access and use matrix context values in steps


<details>
<summary>🔢 Scenario 10-A: Multi-Dimensional Matrix with `include` and `exclude`</summary>


**Objective:** Build a test matrix across operating systems and Node.js versions, exclude an unsupported combination, and add an `include` entry that injects extra variables for a specific combination.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–9 complete; Node.js conceptual understanding helpful but not required.

**Context:** The `strategy.matrix` key generates multiple parallel job instances from combinations of values. Multi-dimensional matrices grow quickly (3 OSes × 4 versions = 12 jobs), so `exclude` and `fail-fast` are essential for cost and correctness. The `include` key adds extra context variables to specific matrix cells without adding new combinations.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/matrix-demo.yml`:

```yaml
name: Matrix Demo

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ${{ matrix.os }}

    strategy:
      # When true (default), all jobs cancel if any one fails
      fail-fast: false
      # Maximum parallel jobs (reduce to control costs)
      max-parallel: 4

      matrix:
        os: [ubuntu-latest, windows-latest, macos-latest]
        node-version: ['18', '20', '22']

        # Exclude a specific combination
        exclude:
          - os: macos-latest
            node-version: '18'  # Node 18 not tested on macOS (hypothetical constraint)

        # Add extra data to a specific combination
        include:
          - os: ubuntu-latest
            node-version: '20'
            # Extra variable only available in this specific cell
            run-integration-tests: true
            label: 'primary'

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Setup Node.js
        uses: actions/setup-node@v4
        with:
          node-version: ${{ matrix.node-version }}

      - name: Print matrix context
        run: |
          echo "OS              : ${{ matrix.os }}"
          echo "Node version    : ${{ matrix.node-version }}"
          echo "Run integration : ${{ matrix.run-integration-tests }}"
          echo "Label           : ${{ matrix.label }}"

      - name: Run integration tests (only on labeled primary cell)
        if: matrix.run-integration-tests == true
        run: echo "Running integration tests on ${{ matrix.os }} / Node ${{ matrix.node-version }}"

      - name: Verify Node version
        run: node --version
```

2. Trigger via `workflow_dispatch` and count the jobs: should be 8 (3×3=9 minus 1 excluded macOS/Node18).
3. Verify only the `ubuntu-latest`/`node-20` job runs the integration test step.
4. Verify `matrix.run-integration-tests` and `matrix.label` are empty in all other cells.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Total jobs = 8 (9 combinations minus 1 excluded).
- [ ] macOS + Node 18 combination does not appear in the run.
- [ ] Only the `ubuntu-latest`/`node-20` job runs the integration test step.
- [ ] `fail-fast: false` means other jobs continue when one fails.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`matrix.run-integration-tests` is empty in most cells** → This is correct and expected; `include` extra keys are only available in the specific cell they are added to.
- **Jobs exceed `max-parallel`** → GitHub queues jobs above the limit; they run when a slot becomes available.
- **`exclude` not working** → The `exclude` object must match the exact combination (all keys must match). A partial match does not exclude.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>⚡ Scenario 10-B: Fail-Fast Behavior and Matrix Output Aggregation</summary>


**Objective:** Observe `fail-fast` behavior, understand when to disable it, and aggregate outputs from multiple matrix jobs into a single downstream job.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 10-A complete.

**Context:** `fail-fast: true` (the default) cancels all running and pending matrix jobs when any one fails. This is useful for expensive jobs but harmful when you want to see all failure results (e.g., cross-platform test results). Matrix outputs require the `fromJSON` function to aggregate across parallel jobs.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/matrix-failfast.yml`:

```yaml
name: Matrix Fail-Fast and Outputs

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  build-matrix:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    strategy:
      fail-fast: false  # collect all results even if some fail
      matrix:
        service: [api, frontend, worker]
    outputs:
      # Capture a matrix output — only last write wins; use artifact for full aggregation
      api-status: ${{ steps.status.outputs.result }}
    steps:
      - name: Simulate build
        id: status
        run: |
          echo "Building ${{ matrix.service }}..."
          if [ "${{ matrix.service }}" == "worker" ]; then
            echo "result=failed" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
            exit 1  # worker build fails intentionally
          fi
          echo "result=success" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

  report:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: build-matrix
    # Run even if some matrix jobs failed
    if: always()
    steps:
      - name: Report matrix results
        run: |
          echo "Build matrix conclusion: ${{ needs.build-matrix.result }}"
          echo "Note: needs.<job>.result is the aggregate across all matrix cells"
          echo "Possible values: success, failure, cancelled, skipped"

          if [ "${{ needs.build-matrix.result }}" == "failure" ]; then
            echo "❌ One or more builds failed"
          else
            echo "✅ All builds passed"
          fi
```

2. Trigger and observe: `api` and `frontend` succeed, `worker` fails.
3. With `fail-fast: false`, the `api` and `frontend` jobs should continue to completion.
4. `report` job runs due to `if: always()` and correctly reports `failure`.
5. Change `fail-fast: true`, re-run — observe `api` or `frontend` may be cancelled mid-run.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] With `fail-fast: false`: all 3 jobs run to their natural conclusion.
- [ ] With `fail-fast: true`: surviving jobs are cancelled when `worker` fails.
- [ ] `needs.build-matrix.result` = `failure` when any cell fails.
- [ ] `report` job executes due to `if: always()` regardless of matrix outcome.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`fail-fast: true` doesn't cancel other jobs quickly** → Cancellation is not instantaneous; jobs that are already running will complete their current step first.
- **`needs.build-matrix.result` is `'success'` despite a failure** → Verify `fail-fast: false`; with `fail-fast: true`, the cancelled jobs may cause the aggregate to report differently depending on timing.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 14 (Inter-Job Communication) for artifact-based aggregation of matrix results; → Section 26 (Troubleshooting) for diagnosing matrix job failures in complex pipelines.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🧮 Scenario 10-C: Dynamic Matrix Generation and Large Matrix Optimization</summary>


**Objective:** Generate matrix values programmatically and optimize large matrices for performance and cost.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 10-B complete; understanding of matrix expansion.

**Context:** Static matrices can explode: 5 versions × 3 OS × 4 test-suites = 60 jobs. Learning dynamic generation and optimization prevents runaway costs.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/dynamic-matrix.yml`:

```yaml
jobs:
  setup:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      matrix: ${{ steps.set.outputs.matrix }}
    steps:
      - id: set
        run: |
          MATRIX=$(echo '{"node": ["18", "20"], "os": ["ubuntu-latest", "windows-latest"]}' | jq -c .)
          echo "matrix=$MATRIX" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

  test:
    needs: setup
    strategy:
      matrix: ${{ fromJSON(needs.setup.outputs.matrix) }}
    runs-on: ${{ matrix.os }}
    steps:
      - run: echo "Testing Node ${{ matrix.node }}"
```

2. Matrix expands to 4 jobs (2 versions × 2 OS).
3. Add `exclude` to remove unwanted combinations.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Matrix generated dynamically from JSON output
- [ ] Job count matches expansion (4 jobs)
- [ ] Exclude filters work to reduce job count

**Time Estimate:** 20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 25 (Performance) for cost optimization; → Section 28 (Practice) for real-world matrix scenarios.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🐛 Scenario 10-D: Fix Matrix Expansion Bugs and False Failures</summary>

```yaml
jobs:
  test:
    strategy:
      matrix:
        os: [ubuntu-latest, windows-latest]
    runs-on: ${{ matrix.os }}
    steps:
      - run: cat file.txt | grep "pattern"  # Fails on Windows (different line endings)
```

2. Fix: use cross-platform command or shell setting.
3. Verify all matrix jobs pass.

**Success Criteria:**

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create matrix with multiple dimensions:

```yaml
strategy:
  matrix:
    team: [frontend, backend, infra]
    test-type: [unit, integration]
```

2. This creates 6 jobs (3 teams × 2 types).
3. Upload results per job.
4. Create aggregation job to merge results.

### 🐳 Section 11: Service Containers & Dependencies (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [04-Workflow-File-Structure.md](04-Workflow-File-Structure.md) (services section)
**Overview:**

- Service containers for testing (databases, Redis, etc.)
- Container configuration: image, ports, options, volumes
- Port mapping and network connectivity
- Health checks and startup conditions
- Environment variables within containers
- Service container networking best practices

**Practical Work:**

- Set up PostgreSQL service container for integration tests
- Configure Redis service container
- Implement health checks for service readiness
- Test application code against service containers
- Debug service container networking issues


<details>
<summary>🐘 Scenario 11-A: PostgreSQL Service Container for Integration Tests</summary>


**Objective:** Spin up a PostgreSQL service container within a workflow job and run database integration tests against it.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–10 complete; Docker conceptual understanding helpful; PostgreSQL `psql` client familiarity useful.

**Context:** Service containers run as Docker containers alongside the job runner. They are available on the job's Docker network and are accessible using `localhost` (or `127.0.0.1`) on the mapped port. Health checks prevent the job steps from running before the service is ready.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/service-containers.yml`:

```yaml
name: Service Container Demo

on: [push, workflow_dispatch]

jobs:
  integration-tests:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest

    services:
      postgres:
        image: postgres:15-alpine
        env:
          POSTGRES_USER: testuser
          POSTGRES_PASSWORD: testpassword
          POSTGRES_DB: testdb
        ports:
          # Maps host port 5432 to container port 5432
          - 5432:5432
        options: >-
          --health-cmd pg_isready
          --health-interval 10s
          --health-timeout 5s
          --health-retries 5

      redis:
        image: redis:7-alpine
        ports:
          - 6379:6379
        options: >-
          --health-cmd "redis-cli ping"
          --health-interval 10s
          --health-timeout 5s
          --health-retries 5

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Wait for services (guaranteed by health checks above)
        run: |
          echo "Services are ready — health checks passed before steps execute"

      - name: Test PostgreSQL connectivity
        env:
          PGPASSWORD: testpassword
        run: |
          # psql is preinstalled on ubuntu-latest runners
          psql -h localhost -U testuser -d testdb -c "SELECT version();"

      - name: Create a test table and insert data
        env:
          PGPASSWORD: testpassword
        run: |
          psql -h localhost -U testuser -d testdb -c "
            CREATE TABLE users (id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY, name TEXT NOT NULL);
            INSERT INTO users (name) VALUES ('Alice'), ('Bob');
            SELECT COUNT(*) FROM users;
          "

      - name: Test Redis connectivity
        run: |
          redis-cli -h localhost -p 6379 SET testkey "hello"
          redis-cli -h localhost -p 6379 GET testkey

      - name: Run application integration test (simulated)
        env:
          DATABASE_URL: postgres://testuser:testpassword@localhost:5432/testdb
          REDIS_URL: redis://localhost:6379
        run: |
          echo "App would connect to: ${DATABASE_URL}"
          echo "Cache would connect to: ${REDIS_URL}"
          echo "All integration tests passed"
```

2. Push to trigger the workflow (or use `workflow_dispatch`).
3. Verify the `postgres` and `redis` service containers appear in the run log under "Initialize containers".
4. Confirm the health check steps pass before job steps execute.
5. Verify `SELECT COUNT(*) FROM users;` returns `2`.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Both service containers start and pass health checks before steps run.
- [ ] PostgreSQL is reachable at `localhost:5432` from job steps.
- [ ] Redis responds to `PING` and can store/retrieve values.
- [ ] Integration test step uses `DATABASE_URL` and `REDIS_URL` environment variables.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`psql: error: connection refused`** → Health check ensures the container is ready, but `psql` must be installed on the runner. Ubuntu-latest includes it; Windows runners do not. Add `apt-get install -y postgresql-client` if using a minimal runner image.
- **Service container port conflict** → Use `0:5432` (random host port) to avoid conflicts, then read the port from `job.services.postgres.ports['5432']`. For simplicity, stick to explicit ports in testing environments.
- **Health check times out** → Increase `--health-retries` or `--health-interval` for slow-starting images.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🐳 Scenario 11-B: Container Jobs — Running the Entire Job Inside a Container</summary>


**Objective:** Use `container:` at the job level to run all steps inside a Docker container, and understand how this differs from service containers.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 11-A complete.

**Context:** The job-level `container:` key runs the entire job inside a specified Docker image. Steps execute inside that container rather than on the runner's OS. This is ideal for guaranteeing a consistent runtime environment without relying on pre-installed runner tools.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/container-job.yml`:

```yaml
name: Container Job Demo

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  build-in-container:
    # All steps run inside this container image
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    container:
      image: node:20-alpine
      # Optionally set environment variables for the container
      env:
        NODE_ENV: test
      # Volume mounts if needed
      # volumes:
      #   - /tmp/cache:/cache

    services:
      # Service containers can still be used alongside container jobs
      redis:
        image: redis:7-alpine
        options: --health-cmd "redis-cli ping" --health-interval 10s --health-retries 5

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Verify we are running inside the container
        run: |
          echo "Node version: $(node --version)"
          echo "npm version: $(npm --version)"
          # Alpine Linux — not the runner's Ubuntu
          cat /etc/os-release | head -3

      - name: Install dependencies
        run: npm install --if-present

      - name: Test Redis from container job
        run: |
          # Inside a container job, service containers are accessed by their service name, not localhost
          # The service name 'redis' becomes the hostname
          apk add --no-cache redis
          redis-cli -h redis -p 6379 PING

      - name: Run tests
        run: |
          echo "Tests would run here inside Node 20 Alpine"
          node -e "console.log('Hello from container job!')"
```

2. Trigger and observe that all steps execute inside `node:20-alpine`.
3. Note the key difference: service containers in container jobs are accessed via their **service name as hostname** (e.g., `redis`), not `localhost`.
4. Compare this to Scenario 11-A where service containers used `localhost`.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Steps show Alpine Linux in the OS release (not Ubuntu).
- [ ] Node 20 is available without a `setup-node` step.
- [ ] Redis is accessed via `redis` hostname (service name), not `localhost`.

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 20 (GitHub-Hosted Runners) for understanding the runner environment; → Section 21 (Self-Hosted Runners) for using container jobs with self-hosted runners where Docker must be available.

</details>

---
