<details>
<summary>🏢 Scenario 22-A: Organization Starter Workflows</summary>


**Objective:** Create an organization-level starter workflow that appears in the "Actions" tab of new repositories, providing a standardized CI template.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–21 complete; organization owner or admin access.

**Context:** Starter workflows are stored in a special `.github` repository within an organization. They appear as template options when a new workflow is created in any repo in the org. They can include reusable workflow calls, enforcing standards across all projects.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create (or use) a `.github` repository in your organization.
2. Create the directory structure:

```text
.github/
  workflow-templates/
    node-ci.yml
    node-ci.properties.json
```

3. Create `.github/workflow-templates/node-ci.yml`:

```yaml
name: Node.js CI

on:
  push:
    branches: [$default-branch]
  pull_request:
    branches: [$default-branch]

# Organization standard: require explicit permissions
permissions:
  contents: read
  pull-requests: write

jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    strategy:
      matrix:
        node-version: [18.x, 20.x]
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Setup Node.js ${{ matrix.node-version }}
        uses: actions/setup-node@v4
        with:
          node-version: ${{ matrix.node-version }}
          cache: npm

      - name: Install dependencies
        run: npm ci

      - name: Run tests
        run: npm test

      - name: Upload coverage
        uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4
        if: always()
        with:
          name: coverage-${{ matrix.node-version }}
          path: coverage/
```

4. Create `.github/workflow-templates/node-ci.properties.json`:

```json
{
  "name": "Node.js CI",
  "description": "Build and test a Node.js project with npm — Org Standard",
  "iconName": "example-icon",
  "categories": [
    "Continuous integration",
    "JavaScript"
  ]
}
```

5. Verify the template appears in a new repository under **Actions → New workflow**.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Template appears in the org's "Actions → New workflow" screen.
- [ ] Template includes `$default-branch` placeholder.
- [ ] `properties.json` provides correct name and description.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Template not appearing** → The `.github` repository must be named exactly `.github` (lowercase). The files must be in `workflow-templates/` at the root.
- **`$default-branch` not replaced** → This is a literal template variable replaced by GitHub when the user applies the template. It works only as a starter workflow, not when run directly.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>📋 Scenario 22-B: Organization Policy Management and Audit Logging</summary>


**Objective:** Configure which actions are allowed in an organization, set required workflows, and query audit logs for workflow activity.

**Context:** Organization owners can restrict which actions run: allow all, allow GitHub actions only, allow selected marketplaces, or specify exact patterns. Required workflows enforce certain checks on all repositories in the org.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Configure action policies (conceptual — requires org admin):
   - **Organization → Settings → Actions → General → Actions permissions**
   - Set: "Allow enterprise, and select non-enterprise, actions and reusable workflows"
   - In the "Allow specified actions and reusable workflows" box, specify a policy:

```text
# Allow only GitHub-owned actions and specific third-party actions:
actions/*,
github/*,
docker/*@v2,
aws-actions/configure-aws-credentials@*
```

2. Query audit logs via GitHub API:

```yaml
name: Audit Log Query

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  audit:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Query recent Actions audit events
        env:
          GH_TOKEN: ${{ github.token }}
        run: |
          # Query GraphQL audit log (requires org:read or audit_log:read scope)
          echo "Querying audit log for Actions events..."
          gh api graphql -f query='
            query {
              organization(login: "YOUR_ORG") {
                auditLog(first: 10, query: "action:workflows") {
                  nodes {
                    ... on WorkflowsWorkflow {
                      actor
                      action
                      createdAt
                    }
                  }
                }
              }
            }
          ' --jq '.data.organization.auditLog.nodes[]'
```

3. Review what a `required_workflow` looks like when configured via the API:

```yaml
      - name: List required workflows
        env:
          GH_TOKEN: ${{ github.token }}
        run: |
          # Required workflows are org-level policies
          gh api /orgs/YOUR_ORG/actions/required_workflows \
            --jq '.required_workflows[] | "\(.name): \(.workflow_file_path)"'
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Action allow-list prevents unauthorized third-party actions from running.
- [ ] Audit log query returns workflow-related events.
- [ ] Required workflow concept is understood and documented.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 21 (Runner Groups) for org-level runner access control; → Section 24 (Security) for the security rationale behind action policy restrictions.

</details>

---

## 🔒 Week 7: Security & Optimization

### 🛡️ Section 23: GitHub Actions Security Best Practices - Part 1 (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [18-Security-and-Optimization.md](18-Security-and-Optimization.md) (first half)
**Overview:**

- Principle of least privilege for permissions
- GITHUB_TOKEN: scope, ephemeral nature, permissions
- Environment protection rules and approval gates
- Script injection prevention techniques
- Input sanitization and validation
- Avoiding untrusted data in shell commands
- Token security and credential management

**Practical Work:**

- Review GITHUB_TOKEN permissions
- Implement least-privilege permissions in workflows
- Sanitize user inputs to prevent injection
- Set up approval gates for sensitive deployments
- Audit workflow permissions


<details>
<summary>🛡️ Scenario 23-A: Least-Privilege GITHUB_TOKEN and Input Sanitization</summary>


**Objective:** Implement explicit minimal permissions, prevent script injection from untrusted PR data, and set up environment approval gates.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–22 complete; understanding of the `permissions:` key (Section 7).

**Context:** The most critical security control in GitHub Actions is preventing script injection. This occurs when untrusted data (PR titles, issue bodies, commit messages from forks) is interpolated directly into `run:` shell commands using `${{ github.event.pull_request.title }}`. The attacker can craft a PR title containing `"; cat /etc/passwd; echo "` to exfiltrate data.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/secure-permissions.yml`:

```yaml
name: Secure Permissions Demo

on:
  pull_request:
    types: [opened, synchronize]

# Default: restrict all permissions — grant only what's needed
permissions:
  contents: read  # checkout only

jobs:
  pr-validation:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    # Override job-level if different from workflow-level
    permissions:
      contents: read
      pull-requests: write  # needed to post PR comments
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      # BAD PATTERN — VULNERABLE TO INJECTION
      # Do NOT do this:
      # - run: echo "PR title is ${{ github.event.pull_request.title }}"

      # GOOD PATTERN — safe interpolation via environment variable
      - name: Safe use of PR metadata
        env:
          PR_TITLE: ${{ github.event.pull_request.title }}
          PR_BODY: ${{ github.event.pull_request.body }}
          PR_AUTHOR: ${{ github.event.pull_request.user.login }}
        run: |
          # Now $PR_TITLE is an environment variable, not shell-interpolated
          echo "PR Author: $PR_AUTHOR"
          echo "PR Title : $PR_TITLE"
          # Safe to use in shell because it's an env var, not inline expression
          if [[ "$PR_TITLE" =~ ^\[BREAKING\] ]]; then
            echo "Breaking change detected — requires senior review"
            exit 1
          fi

      - name: Validate PR branch naming
        env:
          BRANCH_NAME: ${{ github.head_ref }}
        run: |
          # Validate branch name follows convention
          if [[ ! "$BRANCH_NAME" =~ ^(feature|bugfix|hotfix|chore)/ ]]; then
            echo "::error::Branch name '$BRANCH_NAME' does not follow naming convention"
            echo "Expected: feature/, bugfix/, hotfix/, or chore/"
            exit 1
          fi
          echo "Branch name valid: $BRANCH_NAME"

      - name: Post PR comment
        env:
          GH_TOKEN: ${{ github.token }}
        run: |
          gh pr comment ${{ github.event.number }} \
            --repo ${{ github.repository }} \
            --body "PR validation passed. Branch naming convention verified."
```

2. Test by creating a PR from a branch named `feature/my-new-feature`.
3. Test with a branch named `bad-branch-name` and observe the error.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Workflow has explicit `permissions:` at both workflow and job level.
- [ ] PR title/body are accessed via environment variables (not inline expressions in `run:`).
- [ ] Branch naming validation catches non-conforming names.
- [ ] `pull-requests: write` allows the comment step to succeed.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`gh pr comment` fails with "Resource not accessible by integration"** → The `pull-requests: write` permission must be explicitly set. For fork PRs, this permission is automatically restricted to read-only.
- **Environment variable approach doesn't work for matrix** → For matrix-interpolated values, use `env:` at the step level to safely pass matrix values to shell.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔐 Scenario 23-B: Environment Protection Rules and Approval Gates</summary>


**Objective:** Configure a protected environment requiring manual approval before deployment, and test that the approval gate blocks automated deployment.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Navigate to **Repository → Settings → Environments → New environment** → name it `production`.
2. Configure protection rules:
   - Required reviewers: add yourself
   - Wait timer: 5 minutes (optional, for testing)
   - Deployment branches: restrict to `main` only

3. Create `.github/workflows/protected-deploy.yml`:

```yaml
name: Protected Deployment

on:
  push:
    branches: [main]

jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      version: ${{ steps.ver.outputs.v }}
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - id: ver
        run: echo "v=1.0.${{ github.run_number }}" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

  deploy-staging:
    needs: build
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment: staging  # no protection rules
    steps:
      - run: echo "Deployed ${{ needs.build.outputs.version }} to staging"

  deploy-production:
    needs: deploy-staging
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment: production  # PROTECTED — requires approval
    permissions:
      contents: read
      id-token: write  # for OIDC if needed
    steps:
      - run: echo "Deployed ${{ needs.build.outputs.version }} to production"

      - name: Record deployment
        run: |
          echo "Version : ${{ needs.build.outputs.version }}"
          echo "Actor   : ${{ github.actor }}"
          echo "SHA     : ${{ github.sha }}"
```

4. Push to `main` and verify:
   - Staging deploys automatically
   - Production is **waiting for approval** in the Actions UI
   - After approval, production deploy proceeds

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Staging deploys without approval.
- [ ] Production shows "Waiting for approval" in the Actions UI.
- [ ] After approval by a required reviewer, production deploy completes.
- [ ] Deployment from non-`main` branches is blocked.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 8 (Environments) for full environment configuration; → Section 24 (Security Part 2) for OIDC-based credential security replacing long-lived secrets.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔄 Scenario 23-C: Secret Rotation and Expiration Policies</summary>

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 23-B complete.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Automate secret rotation every 90 days
2. Set expiration on organization secrets

---

<details>
<summary>🔑 Scenario 23-D: OIDC Token-Based Authentication</summary>

### 🔒 Section 24: GitHub Actions Security Best Practices - Part 2 (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [18-Security-and-Optimization.md](18-Security-and-Optimization.md) (second half)
**Overview:**

- Action pinning: commit SHA vs tags vs fuzzy versions
- Verifying action sources and trustworthiness
- OIDC tokens for cloud provider federation
- Artifact attestation and provenance (SLSA, build metadata)
- Deployment verification workflows
- Secrets scanning and rotation
- Vulnerability scanning in workflows

**Practical Work:**

- Pin third-party actions to commit SHAs
- Implement OIDC for AWS/Azure scenarios
- Verify artifact provenance
- Set up secrets scanning
- Implement dependency scanning


<details>
<summary>📌 Scenario 24-A: Pin Actions to Commit SHAs and Implement OIDC</summary>


**Objective:** Convert all action references from mutable tags to immutable commit SHAs, and implement OIDC federation with a cloud provider to eliminate long-lived credentials.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–23 complete; AWS or Azure account (or use simulated OIDC steps).

**Context:** Pinning to a commit SHA (`uses: actions/checkout@11bd71901bbe5b1630ceea73d27597364c9af683`) prevents supply chain attacks where a malicious actor takes over an action's GitHub account and pushes malicious code to the `@v4` tag. OIDC (OpenID Connect) allows workflows to authenticate to cloud providers using short-lived, automatically rotated tokens — no secrets stored in GitHub.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create a workflow demonstrating SHA pinning vs tag pinning:

```yaml
name: Security Best Practices

on: [push]

# Minimal permissions at workflow level
permissions: {}

jobs:
  secure-workflow:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    permissions:
      contents: read      # for checkout
      id-token: write     # for OIDC token request

    steps:
      # BAD: mutable tag — can be changed by action author
      # - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      # GOOD: pinned to exact commit SHA
      - uses: actions/checkout@11bd71901bbe5b1630ceea73d27597364c9af683  # v4.2.2

      # GOOD: also pin third-party actions to SHA
      - uses: actions/setup-node@39370e3970a6d050c480ffad4ff0ed4d3fdee5af  # v4.1.0
        with:
          node-version: '20'

      - name: Show commit SHA verification
        run: |
          echo "Current commit: ${{ github.sha }}"
          echo "Actions are pinned to immutable SHAs"

      # OIDC: Request a token from GitHub and exchange for cloud provider credentials
      - name: Configure AWS credentials via OIDC
        # In production: uses: aws-actions/configure-aws-credentials@SHA
        # Here we simulate the OIDC token request
        run: |
          echo "Requesting OIDC token..."
          # The id-token permission allows this API call
          OIDC_TOKEN=$(curl -sS \
            -H "Authorization: bearer $ACTIONS_ID_TOKEN_REQUEST_TOKEN" \
            "$ACTIONS_ID_TOKEN_REQUEST_URL&audience=sts.amazonaws.com" \
            | jq -r '.value')
          echo "OIDC token acquired (length: ${#OIDC_TOKEN})"
          echo "Token payload (claims):"
          echo $OIDC_TOKEN | cut -d. -f2 | base64 -d 2>/dev/null | jq . || echo "Decode requires padding"
```

2. To find the SHA for any action:

```bash
# Method 1: From GitHub API
gh api repos/actions/checkout/git/ref/tags/v4 --jq '.object.sha'

# Method 2: From the Actions Marketplace page — click the tag, copy SHA from URL
# Method 3: From the repo commit history
```

3. Configure OIDC trust (concept — requires cloud provider):

```text
# AWS: IAM OIDC Identity Provider
# Provider URL: https://token.actions.githubusercontent.com
# Audience: sts.amazonaws.com
#
# Trust policy condition:
# "token.actions.githubusercontent.com:sub": "repo:ORG/REPO:ref:refs/heads/main"
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] All `uses:` references include a SHA comment showing the equivalent tag.
- [ ] `id-token: write` permission enables OIDC token request.
- [ ] OIDC token is successfully acquired and its claims are decoded.
- [ ] `permissions: {}` at workflow level ensures jobs must explicitly opt-in.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`$ACTIONS_ID_TOKEN_REQUEST_TOKEN` is empty** → The `id-token: write` permission must be set at the job level, not just workflow level. Also, this only works on GitHub-hosted runners by default.
- **SHA pinning breaks `dependabot`** → Configure Dependabot for GitHub Actions: it can automatically update pinned SHA references.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔍 Scenario 24-B: Artifact Attestation and Dependency Scanning</summary>


**Objective:** Generate an artifact attestation (provenance) for a build artifact and scan workflow dependencies for vulnerabilities.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/attestation.yml`:

```yaml
name: Build with Attestation

on: [push]

permissions:
  contents: read
  id-token: write     # required for attestation signing
  attestations: write # required to write attestations

jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@11bd71901bbe5b1630ceea73d27597364c9af683  # v4.2.2

      - name: Build artifact
        run: |
          echo "Build output" > my-artifact.tar.gz
          sha256sum my-artifact.tar.gz

      - name: Generate artifact attestation
        uses: actions/attest-build-provenance@210c1913531a0be71a26e72c5be3c7ea1c43f47f  # v2.0.0
        with:
          subject-path: my-artifact.tar.gz

      - name: Upload artifact
        uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4
        with:
          name: my-artifact
          path: my-artifact.tar.gz
```

2. After the run, verify the attestation:

```bash
gh attestation verify my-artifact.tar.gz \
  --repo OWNER/REPO
```

3. Add a dependency scanning step:

```yaml
      - name: Scan for dependency vulnerabilities
        run: |
          # Using npm audit for Node.js projects
          npm audit --audit-level=high || true
          # Or: using Trivy for container/filesystem scanning
          # trivy fs --exit-code 1 --severity HIGH,CRITICAL .
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] `attest-build-provenance` action creates a provenance attestation in the repository.
- [ ] `gh attestation verify` confirms the artifact's integrity.
- [ ] `attestations: write` permission is required and set.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 19 (Publishing Actions) for understanding why action consumers should pin to SHAs; → Section 22 (Enterprise) for org-level policies requiring SHA pinning.

</details>

### ⚡ Section 25: Workflow Performance Optimization (2 hrs)

**Topics:** [10-Workflow-Caching.md](10-Workflow-Caching.md), [18-Security-and-Optimization.md](18-Security-and-Optimization.md) (optimization section)
**Overview:**

- Identifying performance bottlenecks
- Caching strategy for build artifacts
- Parallel job execution and concurrency
- Matrix configuration optimization
- Container image layer caching
- Artifact size management
- Retention policy optimization for cost

**Practical Work:**

- Profile slow workflows
- Implement caching improvements
- Reduce artifact sizes
- Parallelize jobs appropriately
- Measure performance improvements


<details>
<summary>📊 Scenario 25-A: Profile and Optimize a Slow Workflow</summary>


**Objective:** Create a deliberately slow workflow, measure step durations, implement caching and parallelization, then compare before/after timings.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–24 complete; Section 9 (Caching) knowledge.

**Context:** The GitHub Actions UI shows step duration in the job log, but doesn't provide a summary view. By adding timing steps and using workflow run data, you can identify bottlenecks. Key optimizations: caching (Section 9), parallel jobs, `needs:` dependency reduction, and concurrency controls.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create a "slow baseline" workflow to measure:

```yaml
name: Performance Baseline

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  slow-sequential:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: TIMER START
        run: echo "Start: $(date +%s)" > /tmp/start.txt

      - name: Simulate: Install dependencies (slow, no cache)
        run: |
          # Simulate uncached npm install
          sleep 15  # typical uncached install time
          echo "Dependencies installed"

      - name: Simulate: Lint
        run: sleep 10

      - name: Simulate: Test suite A
        run: sleep 20

      - name: Simulate: Test suite B
        run: sleep 20

      - name: TIMER END
        run: |
          START=$(cat /tmp/start.txt | cut -d: -f2)
          END=$(date +%s)
          echo "Total time: $((END - START)) seconds"
```

2. Run and note the total time (~65+ seconds due to sequential steps).

3. Create the optimized version:

```yaml
name: Performance Optimized

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  # Install dependencies and cache them
  setup:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      cache-hit: ${{ steps.cache.outputs.cache-hit }}
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Cache dependencies
        id: cache
        uses: actions/cache@v4
        with:
          path: node_modules
          key: ${{ runner.os }}-node-${{ hashFiles('**/package-lock.json') }}
          restore-keys: |
            ${{ runner.os }}-node-

      - name: Install if cache missed
        if: steps.cache.outputs.cache-hit != 'true'
        run: sleep 15  # simulated install

  # Lint and test suites run in parallel
  lint:
    needs: setup
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/cache@v4
        with:
          path: node_modules
          key: ${{ runner.os }}-node-${{ hashFiles('**/package-lock.json') }}
      - run: sleep 10  # simulated lint

  test-a:
    needs: setup
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/cache@v4
        with:
          path: node_modules
          key: ${{ runner.os }}-node-${{ hashFiles('**/package-lock.json') }}
      - run: sleep 20  # simulated test suite A

  test-b:
    needs: setup
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/cache@v4
        with:
          path: node_modules
          key: ${{ runner.os }}-node-${{ hashFiles('**/package-lock.json') }}
      - run: sleep 20  # simulated test suite B

  done:
    needs: [lint, test-a, test-b]
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: echo "All checks passed — parallel execution complete"
```

4. Compare run times: the optimized version runs lint and both test suites in parallel, reducing wall-clock time from ~65s to ~35s (setup + max of parallel jobs).

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Baseline workflow takes ~65 seconds.
- [ ] Optimized workflow takes ~35 seconds (setup 15s + parallel 20s = ~35s vs 65s).
- [ ] Cache hit on second run reduces setup to ~2 seconds.
- [ ] GitHub Actions UI shows parallel job execution in the dependency graph view.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Cache key produces no hit** → Ensure `package-lock.json` exists. The `hashFiles()` function returns an empty string if no files match, causing cache misses.
- **Parallel jobs have no shared cache** → Each parallel job must restore the cache independently. The cache is shared across jobs by key.

**Time Estimate:** 30–35 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>⚙️ Scenario 25-B: Concurrency Controls and Artifact Size Optimization</summary>


**Objective:** Implement concurrency groups to cancel in-progress runs on new pushes, and reduce artifact retention and size.

**Detailed Steps:**

```yaml
name: Concurrency Optimized CI

on:
  push:
    branches: [main, 'feature/**']
  pull_request:

# Cancel in-progress runs when a new push arrives on the same branch
concurrency:
  group: ${{ github.workflow }}-${{ github.ref }}
  cancel-in-progress: true  # for feature branches — not for main

jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Build application
        run: |
          echo "Building..."
          mkdir -p dist
          echo "Build output $(date)" > dist/app.js
          echo "More output" >> dist/app.js

      - name: Upload minimal artifact
        uses: actions/upload-artifact@v4
        with:
          name: build-output
          path: dist/
          # Reduce storage costs — keep for 1 day on PRs, 30 days on main
          retention-days: ${{ github.ref == 'refs/heads/main' && 30 || 1 }}
          # Compress artifacts to reduce size
          compression-level: 9
          # Fail if artifact is empty (catches build failures silently outputting nothing)
          if-no-files-found: error
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Rapid pushes to the same branch cancel the in-progress run.
- [ ] `retention-days` varies based on branch.
- [ ] `if-no-files-found: error` catches empty build outputs.

**Time Estimate:** 15–20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 9 (Caching) for the deep dive on cache keys and restore keys; → Section 14 (Matrix) for parallelizing tests across Node.js versions.

</details>

---

### 🔦 Section 26: Troubleshooting & Common Failures - Part 2 (2 hrs)

**Overview:**

<details>
<summary>🐛 Scenario 26-A: Matrix Job Failure Diagnosis and Recovery</summary>


**Objective:** Create a matrix workflow with intentional failures, use `fail-fast: false` to collect all results, implement retry logic, and use `include`/`exclude` to fix matrix edge cases.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–25 complete; Section 14 (Matrix) knowledge.

**Context:** Matrix failures can be subtle: one OS or Node version fails while others pass. With `fail-fast: true` (default), all matrix jobs cancel when one fails, hiding information about which combinations are broken. Setting `fail-fast: false` lets all combinations run, revealing the full failure surface.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/matrix-debug.yml`:

```yaml
name: Matrix Debugging

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  matrix-with-failures:
    strategy:
      fail-fast: false  # collect results from ALL matrix combinations
      matrix:
        os: [ubuntu-latest, windows-latest, macos-latest]
        node: [18, 20]
        # Exclude known incompatible combinations
        exclude:
          - os: windows-latest
            node: 18  # hypothetical incompatibility
        include:
          # Add extra properties to specific combinations
          - os: ubuntu-latest
            node: 20
            experimental: true  # mark as experimental

    runs-on: ${{ matrix.os }}
    continue-on-error: ${{ matrix.experimental == true }}  # don't fail workflow for experimental

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - uses: actions/setup-node@v4
        with:
          node-version: ${{ matrix.node }}

      - name: Platform-specific test
        run: |
          echo "OS  : ${{ matrix.os }}"
          echo "Node: ${{ matrix.node }}"
          # Simulate: macOS + Node 20 sometimes fails
          if [[ "${{ matrix.os }}" == "macos-latest" && "${{ matrix.node }}" == "20" ]]; then
            echo "Simulating intermittent macOS failure"
            exit 1
          fi
          echo "Test passed"
        shell: bash

      - name: Summary output
        if: always()
        run: |
          echo "Matrix cell: ${{ matrix.os }} / Node ${{ matrix.node }}"
          echo "Status: ${{ job.status }}"
```

2. Run and observe in the Actions UI:
   - All non-excluded combinations run
   - `windows-latest/node18` is skipped (excluded)
   - `macos-latest/node20` fails but workflow continues
   - `experimental: true` combination failure doesn't fail the overall workflow

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] `fail-fast: false` allows all matrix combinations to complete.
- [ ] Excluded matrix combination (`windows/node18`) does not appear in the run.
- [ ] `continue-on-error: true` for experimental job prevents workflow failure.
- [ ] Failure message clearly identifies which matrix combination failed.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **`continue-on-error` not working with dynamic expression** → `continue-on-error` supports expressions, but the value must be a boolean. Use `${{ matrix.experimental == true }}` rather than `${{ matrix.experimental }}`.
- **`shell: bash` required on Windows** → Windows runners use PowerShell by default. Add `shell: bash` to use bash syntax cross-platform.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🖥️ Scenario 26-B: Cross-Platform Compatibility and Log Analysis</summary>


**Objective:** Identify and fix common cross-platform issues (path separators, line endings, command availability) and use log analysis patterns to find failure root causes.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create a workflow that surfaces common cross-platform issues:

```yaml
name: Cross-Platform Compatibility

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  cross-platform:
    strategy:
      fail-fast: false
      matrix:
        os: [ubuntu-latest, windows-latest, macos-latest]
    runs-on: ${{ matrix.os }}
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      # Issue 1: Path separators
      - name: Safe path handling
        run: |
          # Use GITHUB_WORKSPACE instead of hardcoded paths
          echo "Workspace: $GITHUB_WORKSPACE"
          # Avoid: /home/runner/work/repo/file.txt (Linux-specific)
          # Use: ${{ github.workspace }}/file.txt (cross-platform)
        shell: bash

      # Issue 2: Line endings (CRLF vs LF)
      - name: Check line endings
        run: |
          # Create a file and check its line endings
          printf "line1\nline2\n" > test.txt
          file test.txt || echo "file command not available"
        shell: bash

      # Issue 3: Command availability
      - name: Safe command usage
        run: |
          # sed, awk, grep available on all platforms when using bash
          echo "hello world" | grep -o "hello"
          echo "1+1" | awk '{print $0}'
        shell: bash

      # Issue 4: Environment variables
      - name: Cross-platform env vars
        run: |
          # Works on all platforms via bash
          export MY_VAR="cross-platform-value"
          echo "MY_VAR=$MY_VAR"
          echo "MY_VAR=${MY_VAR}" >> $GITHUB_ENV
        shell: bash

      - name: Verify env var persists
        run: echo "Persisted: $MY_VAR"
        shell: bash
```

2. Simulate a log analysis workflow that surfaces error patterns:

```yaml
      - name: Log analysis demonstration
        run: |
          # Common patterns to grep in CI logs:
          # 1. Error lines
          echo "Searching for errors..."
          echo "ERROR: connection refused" | grep -i "error\|fail\|exception" && echo "Found issues"
          # 2. Test failure counts
          echo "Tests: 42 passed, 3 failed" | grep -oP '\d+ failed'
          # 3. Slow step detection
          echo "Step completed in 145s" | awk '{ if ($NF > 60) print "SLOW:", $0 }'
        shell: bash
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] All three OS types pass the cross-platform steps using `shell: bash`.
- [ ] Environment variable persists across steps via `$GITHUB_ENV`.
- [ ] Log analysis patterns identify error keywords.

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 15 (Troubleshooting Part 1) for debug logging; → Section 25 (Performance) for identifying slow steps via log analysis.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔄 Scenario 26-C: Advanced Failure Pattern Analysis and Automated Recovery</summary>


**Objective:** Implement ML-style failure pattern detection to automatically recover from known failure modes.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 26-B complete.

**Context:** Enterprise systems encounter recurring failure patterns. Automated recovery reduces manual intervention.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create workflow that logs all failures with structured metadata
2. Parse failure logs to extract error patterns (timeout, rate limit, auth, network)
3. Build decision tree: if pattern matches known failure → execute recovery
4. Test recovery flow end-to-end
5. Validate that retry succeeds after recovery

**Detailed Workflow Example:**

```yaml
name: Failure Pattern Analysis
on:
  workflow_dispatch:
jobs:
  detect-and-recover:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Simulate potential failure
        id: attempt
        continue-on-error: true
        run: |
          # Simulate different failure modes
          FAILURE_MODE=${{ github.event.inputs.failure_mode || 'timeout' }}
          case "$FAILURE_MODE" in
            timeout)
              sleep 65  # Trigger step timeout after 60s
              ;;
            rate-limit)
              for i in {1..50}; do curl -s https://api.github.com/user; done
              ;;
            auth)
              curl -H "Authorization: Bearer invalid" https://api.github.com/user
              ;;
          esac

      - name: Detect failure pattern
        id: pattern
        if: failure()
        run: |
          echo "failure_detected=true" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          # In production: parse logs to detect specific failure
          if [[ "${{ job.status }}" == "failure" ]]; then
            echo "Detected: Step failed, analyzing pattern..."
            # Classify: timeout vs auth vs network
            echo "pattern=timeout" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          fi

      - name: Execute recovery - timeout
        if: steps.pattern.outputs.pattern == 'timeout'
        run: |
          echo "Executing timeout recovery: splitting workload..."
          # Break long-running task into smaller chunks
          echo "Chunk 1 complete"
          echo "Chunk 2 complete"

      - name: Execute recovery - auth
        if: steps.pattern.outputs.pattern == 'auth'
        run: |
          echo "Executing auth recovery: refreshing credentials..."
          # Refresh tokens or re-authenticate
          echo "Credentials refreshed"

      - name: Verify recovery success
        run: echo "Recovery workflow executed successfully"
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Workflow detects and classifies failure pattern
- [ ] Recovery procedure executes automatically on match
- [ ] Retry after recovery succeeds
- [ ] Failure metadata logged for analysis

**Expected Output:**

```bash
Detected: Step failed, analyzing pattern...
pattern=timeout
Executing timeout recovery: splitting workload...
Chunk 1 complete
Chunk 2 complete
Recovery workflow executed successfully
```

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Pattern not detected:** Check log parsing logic; ensure failure metadata captured
- **Recovery doesn't match failure:** Add more pattern classification rules
- **Retry still fails:** Recovery procedure insufficient; escalate to manual intervention

**Time Estimate:** 30–35 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 9 (Conditional Logic) for if-conditions; → Section 15 (Debugging) for log analysis.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>👁️ Scenario 26-D: Workflow Failure Prevention Through Proactive Monitoring</summary>


**Objective:** Implement proactive health checks to prevent failures before they occur.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 26-C complete.

**Context:** Preventing failures is better than recovering from them. Proactive monitoring enables early intervention.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create scheduled workflow that checks system health
2. Monitor dependency availability (APIs, services, databases)
3. Alert if health checks fail
4. Automatically pause dependent workflows if dependencies unhealthy

**Detailed Workflow Example:**

```yaml
name: Proactive Health Monitoring
on:
  schedule:
    - cron: '*/30 * * * *'  # Every 30 minutes
  workflow_dispatch:
jobs:
  health-check:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Check GitHub API health
        id: github-api
        continue-on-error: true
        run: |
          RESPONSE=$(curl -s -w "\n%{http_code}" -H "Authorization: token ${{ secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN }}" \
            https://api.github.com/repos/${{ github.repository }})
          STATUS=$(echo "$RESPONSE" | tail -n 1)
          if [ "$STATUS" != "200" ]; then
            echo "health_status=unhealthy" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
            echo "failing_service=github-api" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          else
            echo "health_status=healthy" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          fi

      - name: Check external dependency
        id: external
        continue-on-error: true
        run: |
          RESPONSE=$(curl -s -w "\n%{http_code}" https://api.github.com/status)
          STATUS=$(echo "$RESPONSE" | tail -n 1)
          if [ "$STATUS" != "200" ]; then
            echo "health_status=unhealthy" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
            echo "failing_service=external-api" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          fi

      - name: Broadcast health status
        run: |
          echo "Health Check Results:"
          echo "  GitHub API: ${{ steps.github-api.outputs.health_status }}"
          echo "  External API: ${{ steps.external.outputs.health_status }}"

      - name: Trigger alert if unhealthy
        if: failure()
        run: |
          echo "ALERT: Dependency health check failed"
          echo "Failing service: ${{ steps.github-api.outputs.failing_service || steps.external.outputs.failing_service }}"
          # In production: send to monitoring system (Slack, PagerDuty, etc.)
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Health checks run on schedule
- [ ] All dependencies monitored
- [ ] Alerts triggered on failure
- [ ] Workflow can pause dependent jobs

**Expected Output:**

```bash
Health Check Results:
  GitHub API: healthy
  External API: healthy
```

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Health checks too slow:** Implement parallel checks
- **False positives:** Adjust timeout and retry logic
- **Alerts not firing:** Verify notification channel configuration

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 5 (Triggers) for scheduled workflows; → Section 24 (Security) for API authentication.

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🏢 Scenario 26-E: Enterprise-Scale Incident Response and Post-Mortems</summary>


**Objective:** Build incident management workflow for large-scale failures with automated post-mortem generation.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 26-D complete.

**Context:** Enterprise systems require structured incident response and learning from failures.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Define incident severity levels
2. Create escalation workflow based on severity
3. Auto-generate post-mortem from logs and metadata
4. Schedule blameless post-mortem review
5. Document lessons learned

**Detailed Workflow Example:**

```yaml
name: Enterprise Incident Response
on:
  workflow_run:
    workflows: [CI, Build, Deploy]
    types: [completed]
  workflow_dispatch:
    inputs:
      incident_severity:
        type: choice
        options: [low, medium, high, critical]
jobs:
  assess-incident:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Determine severity
        id: severity
        run: |
          # Assess from workflow failure context
          if [[ "${{ github.event.workflow_run.conclusion }}" == "failure" ]]; then
            # Simple heuristic: check how many jobs failed
            echo "severity=high" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          else
            echo "severity=low" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          fi

      - name: Escalate if critical
        if: steps.severity.outputs.severity == 'high' || steps.severity.outputs.severity == 'critical'
        run: |
          echo "INCIDENT ESCALATION"
          echo "Severity: ${{ steps.severity.outputs.severity }}"
          echo "Notifying on-call engineer..."
          # In production: PagerDuty API call

  generate-postmortem:
    needs: assess-incident
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Collect incident metadata
        run: |
          cat > incident.json <<EOF
          {
            "timestamp": "$(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ)",
            "workflow_id": "${{ github.event.workflow_run.id }}",
            "workflow_name": "${{ github.event.workflow_run.name }}",
            "conclusion": "${{ github.event.workflow_run.conclusion }}",
            "branch": "${{ github.event.workflow_run.head_branch }}",
            "commit_sha": "${{ github.event.workflow_run.head_commit.id }}"
          }
          EOF
          cat incident.json

      - name: Generate post-mortem template
        run: |
          cat > POST_MORTEM.md <<'EOF'
          # Incident Post-Mortem

          ## Summary
          - **Date:** $(date -u +%Y-%m-%d)
          - **Duration:** [To be determined]
          - **Impact:** [Describe impact to users/services]

          ## Timeline
          - **T+0min:** Incident detected
          - **T+5min:** Alert fired
          - **T+10min:** On-call engineer engaged
          - **T+20min:** Root cause identified
          - **T+30min:** Service restored

          ## Root Cause Analysis
          [Investigation results]

          ## Contributing Factors
          - [Factor 1]
          - [Factor 2]

          ## Resolution Steps
          - [Step 1]
          - [Step 2]

          ## Lessons Learned
          - [What we learned]

          ## Action Items
          - [ ] Implement monitoring for [X]
          - [ ] Update runbook
          - [ ] Schedule training session

          ## Next Steps
          - Review in post-mortem meeting
          - Assign owners to action items
          - Track resolution
          EOF
          cat POST_MORTEM.md

      - name: Create post-mortem issue
        run: |
          echo "Post-mortem generated and ready for review"
          echo "Scheduled for team review in 24 hours"

  notify-team:
    needs: generate-postmortem
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Send notification
        run: |
          echo "Team notification: Incident post-mortem prepared"
          echo "Next post-mortem review: [Scheduled time]"
          # In production: Send to Slack channel
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Incident severity assessed automatically
- [ ] Escalation triggered for high-severity incidents
- [ ] Post-mortem template generated from metadata
- [ ] Team notified
- [ ] Post-mortem scheduled for review

**Expected Output:**

```bash
INCIDENT ESCALATION
Severity: high
Notifying on-call engineer...
Team notification: Incident post-mortem prepared
```

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Severity assessment inaccurate:** Refine heuristics for classification
- **Notifications not sent:** Verify notification channel credentials
- **Post-mortem incomplete:** Add more metadata collection steps

**Time Estimate:** 35–40 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 22 (Enterprise Management) for org-scale processes; → Section 15 (Debugging) for log collection.

</details>

<details>
<summary>📊 Scenario 27-A: Rich Job Summaries with Test and Coverage Reports</summary>


**Objective:** Use `GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY` to generate formatted markdown reports containing test results, coverage percentages, and deployment metadata — visible directly in the Actions UI without needing to download artifacts.

**Prerequisites:** Sections 1–26 complete; Section 12 (Artifacts) knowledge.

**Context:** `GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY` is a special file path that accepts Markdown appended during workflow execution. The content appears in the Actions UI on the workflow run's Summary tab. This replaces the need for external reporting tools for common CI summaries, and unlike artifacts, summaries are retained as part of the run record.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Create `.github/workflows/job-summary.yml`:

```yaml
name: Job Summary Demo

on: workflow_dispatch

jobs:
  test-with-summary:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Run tests (simulated)
        id: test-run
        run: |
          # Simulate test output
          echo "PASSED=42" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          echo "FAILED=3"  >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          echo "SKIPPED=5" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          echo "COVERAGE=78.5" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          # Return non-zero if tests failed — but continue to write summary
          exit 0

      - name: Generate test summary
        if: always()  # run even if previous step failed
        run: |
          PASSED=${{ steps.test-run.outputs.PASSED }}
          FAILED=${{ steps.test-run.outputs.FAILED }}
          SKIPPED=${{ steps.test-run.outputs.SKIPPED }}
          COVERAGE=${{ steps.test-run.outputs.COVERAGE }}

          # Write markdown to GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY
          cat >> $GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY << 'EOF'
          ## 🧪 Test Results

          | Metric    | Value |
          |-----------|-------|
          | ✅ Passed  | 42    |
          | ❌ Failed  | 3     |
          | ⏭️ Skipped | 5     |
          | 📊 Coverage| 78.5% |

          ### Failed Tests
          - `auth/login.test.ts` — AssertionError: expected 401 got 403
          - `api/users.test.ts` — Timeout after 5000ms
          - `utils/format.test.ts` — TypeError: undefined is not a function

          > ⚠️ Coverage is below the 80% threshold.
          EOF

      - name: Generate coverage badge data
        run: |
          COVERAGE=78.5
          # Determine badge color
          if (( $(echo "$COVERAGE >= 80" | bc -l) )); then
            COLOR="brightgreen"
          elif (( $(echo "$COVERAGE >= 60" | bc -l) )); then
            COLOR="yellow"
          else
            COLOR="red"
          fi
          echo "Badge: https://img.shields.io/badge/coverage-${COVERAGE}%25-${COLOR}"

          # Append to summary
          cat >> $GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY << EOF
          ## 📈 Coverage Report

          ![Coverage](https://img.shields.io/badge/coverage-${COVERAGE}%25-${COLOR})

          Coverage dropped below threshold (80%). Review uncovered files:
          - \`src/auth/oauth.ts\` — 45% covered
          - \`src/api/webhooks.ts\` — 52% covered
          EOF

  deployment-summary:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment: staging
    steps:
      - name: Simulate deployment
        id: deploy
        run: |
          echo "sha=${{ github.sha }}" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          echo "version=1.0.${{ github.run_number }}" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          echo "Deployed successfully"

      - name: Write deployment summary
        run: |
          cat >> $GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY << EOF
          ## 🚀 Deployment Summary

          | Field    | Value |
          |----------|-------|
          | Version  | ${{ steps.deploy.outputs.version }} |
          | SHA      | \`${{ github.sha }}\` |
          | Actor    | ${{ github.actor }} |
          | Time     | $(date -u +"%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ") |
          | Status   | ✅ Success |

          [View deployment logs](#) • [Rollback instructions](#)
          EOF
```

2. Run the workflow and navigate to the Actions UI → Summary tab to see the formatted reports.

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Summary tab shows the test results table with counts.
- [ ] Failed test names are listed with error types.
- [ ] Coverage percentage is rendered with the appropriate badge color.
- [ ] Deployment summary shows version, SHA, actor, and timestamp.
- [ ] `if: always()` ensures summary is written even if tests fail.

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Heredoc (`<< 'EOF'`) vs heredoc (`<< EOF`)** → Use `<< 'EOF'` (quoted) to prevent shell expansion inside the block. Use `<< EOF` (unquoted) when you need `$GITHUB_OUTPUT` values to expand.
- **Summary not appearing** → Ensure you're writing to `$GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY` (the file path, not a variable). On Windows runners use `Add-Content $env:GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY "content"`.

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

</details>

---

<details>
<summary>🔖 Scenario 27-B: Status Badges and Emergency Runbook Workflows</summary>


**Objective:** Add workflow status badges to a README and create an emergency runbook workflow that generates an HTML status summary.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Add a status badge to `README.md`:

```markdown
# My Application

![CI Status](https://github.com/OWNER/REPO/actions/workflows/ci.yml/badge.svg)
![CD Status](https://github.com/OWNER/REPO/actions/workflows/deploy.yml/badge.svg?branch=main)
![Security Scan](https://github.com/OWNER/REPO/actions/workflows/security.yml/badge.svg)
```

2. Create an emergency status check workflow:

```yaml
name: Emergency Status Check

on:
  workflow_dispatch:
    inputs:
      environment:
        description: 'Environment to check'
        type: choice
        options: [staging, production]
        required: true

jobs:
  status-check:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Check workflow statuses
        env:
          GH_TOKEN: ${{ github.token }}
        run: |
          echo "Checking recent workflow runs..."
          gh run list \
            --repo ${{ github.repository }} \
            --limit 10 \
            --json name,status,conclusion,headBranch,createdAt \
            --jq '.[] | [.name, .status, (.conclusion // "in_progress"), .headBranch] | @tsv'

      - name: Write emergency summary
        run: |
          cat >> $GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY << EOF
          ## 🚨 Emergency Status Report
          **Environment:** ${{ inputs.environment }}
          **Checked by:** ${{ github.actor }}
          **Time:** $(date -u)

          ### Recent Workflow Status
          $(gh run list --repo ${{ github.repository }} --limit 5 \
            --json name,conclusion \
            --jq '.[] | "- " + .name + ": " + (.conclusion // "running")')
          EOF
        env:
          GH_TOKEN: ${{ github.token }}
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] README badges show live workflow status.
- [ ] Emergency workflow accepts environment selection.
- [ ] Summary contains formatted status data from the GitHub API.

**Time Estimate:** 20 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 12 (Artifacts) for when summaries aren't sufficient and full reports need downloading; → Section 16 (Workflow REST API) for querying workflow run data via API.

</details>

<summary>🎨 Scenario 27-C: Custom Formatting and Structured Output in Job Summaries</summary>


**Objective:** Create rich, formatted job summaries with tables, charts, and custom styling.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 27-B complete.

**Context:** Rich summaries improve visibility and enable better decision-making.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Generate test results in JSON format
2. Parse JSON and create formatted summary with tables
3. Add Mermaid diagrams for visual representation
4. Include collapsible sections for detailed output
5. Format with emojis and markdown styling

**Detailed Workflow Example:**

```yaml
name: Custom Formatted Summary
on: push
jobs:
  test-and-summarize:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Run tests
        id: tests
        run: |
          # Simulate test run
          cat > test-results.json <<'EOF'
          {
            "total": 45,
            "passed": 42,
            "failed": 2,
            "skipped": 1,
            "duration_seconds": 125
          }
          EOF
          cat test-results.json

      - name: Generate custom summary
        run: |
          cat >> $GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY <<'EOF'
          # 📊 Test Results Summary

          ## Quick Stats
          | Metric | Value |
          |--------|-------|
          | Total Tests | 45 |
          | ✅ Passed | 42 |
          | ❌ Failed | 2 |
          | ⏭️ Skipped | 1 |
          | ⏱️ Duration | 125s |

          ## Test Breakdown


          ### Failed Tests
          - `test_auth_token_refresh` - timeout
          - `test_cache_invalidation` - assertion error

          ### Skipped Tests
          - `test_performance_benchmark` - requires special config


          ## Performance Chart
          ```mermaid

          pie title Test Results
            "Passed (42)" : 42
            "Failed (2)" : 2
            "Skipped (1)" : 1

          ```bash

          ---
          **Generated at:** $(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ)
          EOF
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Summary contains formatted table
- [ ] Mermaid diagram displays correctly
- [ ] Collapsible sections expand/collapse
- [ ] Summary visible on workflow run page

**Expected Output:**

Formatted summary with table, pie chart, and collapsible sections

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Markdown not rendering:** Check syntax (tables need pipes, mermaid needs code fence)
- **Diagram not showing:** Verify mermaid syntax is valid
- **Emoji not displaying:** Use escaped unicode sequences

**Time Estimate:** 20–25 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 14 (Inter-Job Communication) for parsing job outputs; → Section 15 (Debugging) for log summaries.

</details>

<details>
<summary>📈 Scenario 27-D: Aggregating Metrics and Dashboards from Multiple Jobs</summary>


**Objective:** Collect metrics from parallel jobs and create unified dashboard views.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 27-C complete.

**Context:** Multi-job workflows generate distributed metrics; aggregation enables holistic visibility.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Each job exports metrics to artifact
2. Collector job reads all metric artifacts
3. Aggregate metrics across jobs
4. Generate unified dashboard view
5. Post dashboard to summary

**Detailed Workflow Example:**

```yaml
name: Multi-Job Metrics Aggregation
on: push
jobs:
  test-suite-1:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: |
          mkdir -p metrics
          echo '{"duration": 45, "tests": 20, "passed": 20}' > metrics/results.json
      - uses: actions/upload-artifact@v3
        with:
          name: metrics-suite-1
          path: metrics/

  test-suite-2:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - run: |
          mkdir -p metrics
          echo '{"duration": 38, "tests": 18, "passed": 17}' > metrics/results.json
      - uses: actions/upload-artifact@v3
        with:
          name: metrics-suite-2
          path: metrics/

  aggregate-and-report:
    needs: [test-suite-1, test-suite-2]
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/download-artifact@v3
        with:
          path: all-metrics

      - name: Aggregate metrics
        run: |
          TOTAL_DURATION=0
          TOTAL_TESTS=0
          TOTAL_PASSED=0

          for file in all-metrics/metrics-*/results.json; do
            DURATION=$(jq '.duration' "$file")
            TESTS=$(jq '.tests' "$file")
            PASSED=$(jq '.passed' "$file")

            TOTAL_DURATION=$((TOTAL_DURATION + DURATION))
            TOTAL_TESTS=$((TOTAL_TESTS + TESTS))
            TOTAL_PASSED=$((TOTAL_PASSED + PASSED))
          done

          echo "total_duration=$TOTAL_DURATION" >> $GITHUB_ENV
          echo "total_tests=$TOTAL_TESTS" >> $GITHUB_ENV
          echo "total_passed=$TOTAL_PASSED" >> $GITHUB_ENV

      - name: Generate aggregated dashboard
        run: |
          cat >> $GITHUB_STEP_SUMMARY <<'EOF'
          # 📊 Aggregated Test Dashboard

          ## Overall Metrics
          | Metric | Value |
          |--------|-------|
          | Total Test Time | ${{ env.total_duration }}s |
          | Total Tests | ${{ env.total_tests }} |
          | Total Passed | ${{ env.total_passed }} |
          | Pass Rate | $(( 100 * ${{ env.total_passed }} / ${{ env.total_tests }} ))% |

          EOF
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Metrics collected from multiple jobs
- [ ] Aggregation computes totals correctly
- [ ] Dashboard displays unified view
- [ ] Percentages and rates calculated

**Expected Output:**

```bash
Aggregated Test Dashboard
Total Test Time: 83s
Total Tests: 38
Total Passed: 37
Pass Rate: 97%
```

**Troubleshooting:**

- **Artifact not found:** Verify upload job names and paths
- **JSON parsing fails:** Check JSON format in uploaded files
- **Math incorrect:** Verify arithmetic in shell script

**Time Estimate:** 25–30 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 12 (Artifacts) for multi-artifact handling; → Section 14 (Outputs) for metrics passing.

</details>

<details>
<summary>📡 Scenario 27-E: Integration with External Reporting and BI Systems</summary>


**Objective:** Export workflow metrics to external BI/analytics platforms for enterprise dashboards.

**Prerequisites:** Scenario 27-D complete.

**Context:** Enterprise teams use centralized BI tools; integrating GitHub workflow data improves visibility.

**Detailed Steps:**

1. Format metrics for BI system (CSV, JSON, SQL)
2. Connect to BI platform API
3. Authenticate and send data
4. Verify data appears in dashboard
5. Create scheduled reporting workflow

**Detailed Workflow Example:**

```yaml
name: Export to BI System
on:
  schedule:
    - cron: '0 */6 * * *'  # Every 6 hours
  workflow_run:
    workflows: [CI]
    types: [completed]
jobs:
  export-metrics:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Collect workflow metrics
        id: metrics
        run: |
          cat > metrics.csv <<'EOF'
          workflow_name,run_id,conclusion,duration_seconds,timestamp
          ${{ github.workflow }},${{ github.run_id }},${{ job.status }},125,$(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ)
          EOF
          cat metrics.csv

      - name: Convert to JSON for API
        id: json
        run: |
          cat > payload.json <<'EOF'
          {
            "workflow": "${{ github.workflow }}",
            "run_id": ${{ github.run_id }},
            "status": "${{ job.status }}",
            "duration_seconds": 125,
            "timestamp": "$(date -u +%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%SZ)",
            "repository": "${{ github.repository }}",
            "branch": "${{ github.ref_name }}"
          }
          EOF
          cat payload.json

      - name: Send to external BI system
        env:
          BI_API_URL: ${{ secrets.BI_API_URL }}
          BI_API_KEY: ${{ secrets.BI_API_KEY }}
        run: |
          curl -X POST "$BI_API_URL/ingest" \
            -H "Authorization: Bearer $BI_API_KEY" \
            -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
            -d @payload.json \
            --fail

      - name: Verify data ingestion
        run: echo "Metrics successfully exported to BI system"

  generate-report:
    needs: export-metrics
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - name: Query BI system for report
        run: |
          echo "Generating weekly workflow report..."
          echo "Total workflows run: 127"
          echo "Average duration: 142 seconds"
          echo "Success rate: 96.8%"
```

**Success Criteria:**

- [ ] Metrics formatted correctly for BI API
- [ ] Authentication successful
- [ ] Data ingestion returns success
- [ ] Data appears in BI dashboard within 5 minutes
- [ ] Scheduled export runs on schedule

**Expected Output:**

```bash
Metrics successfully exported to BI system
Generating weekly workflow report...
Total workflows run: 127
Average duration: 142 seconds
Success rate: 96.8%
```

**Troubleshooting:**

- **API authentication fails:** Verify API key and URL in secrets
- **Data format rejected:** Check BI API documentation for payload format
- **Schedule doesn't trigger:** Verify cron syntax and repo permissions
- **Data not appearing in dashboard:** Check BI system logs for ingestion errors

**Time Estimate:** 30–35 minutes

**Cross-Section Connections:** → Section 16 (REST API) for API integration; → Section 8 (Secrets) for credential management.

</details>
